<p>A <b>caliphate</b> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">خِلافة</span>‎‎ <i>khilāfa</i>) is an area containing an <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islamic</a> <a href="/wiki/Steward_(office)" title="Steward (office)">steward</a> known as a <b>caliph</b> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">خَليفة</span>‎‎ <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="ar ALA transliteration">khalīfah</span></i> <span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:Ar-khalifa.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a> </span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/67/Ar-khalifa.ogg" title="Ar-khalifa.ogg">pronunciation</a></span> <small class="metadata audiolinkinfo" style="cursor:help;">(<a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help"><span style="cursor:help;">help</span></a>·<a href="/wiki/File:Ar-khalifa.ogg" title="File:Ar-khalifa.ogg"><span style="cursor:help;">info</span></a>)</small></span>)—a person considered a religious successor to the <a href="/wiki/Prophets_and_messengers_in_Islam" title="Prophets and messengers in Islam">Islamic prophet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> (<a href="/wiki/Muhammad_in_Islam" title="Muhammad in Islam">Muhammad ibn ʿAbdullāh</a>), and a leader of the entire <a href="/wiki/Ummah" title="Ummah">Muslim community</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-1">[1]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Rashidun_Caliphate" title="Rashidun Caliphate">Rashidun caliphs</a>, who directly <a href="/wiki/Succession_to_Muhammad" title="Succession to Muhammad">succeeded Muhammad</a> as leaders of the <a href="/wiki/Ummah" title="Ummah">Muslim community</a>, were chosen through <i><a href="/wiki/Shura" title="Shura">shura</a>,</i> a process of community consultation that some consider to be an early form of Islamic <a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">democracy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Islam" title="History of Islam">history of Islam</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Rashidun" title="Rashidun">Rashidun</a> period, many <a href="/wiki/List_of_Muslim_empires_and_dynasties" title="List of Muslim empires and dynasties">Muslim states</a>, almost all of them <a href="/wiki/Hereditary_monarchy" title="Hereditary monarchy">hereditary monarchies</a>, have claimed to be caliphates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam" title="Sunni Islam">Sunni</a> branch of Islam stipulates that, as a head of state, a caliph should be <a href="/wiki/Election" title="Election">elected</a> by Muslims or their representatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2muslims_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-2muslims-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (January 2017)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Followers of <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam" title="Shia Islam">Shia Islam</a>, however, believe a caliph should be an <a href="/wiki/Imam" title="Imam">Imam</a> chosen by <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">God</a> from the <i><a href="/wiki/Ahl_al-Bayt" title="Ahl al-Bayt">Ahl al-Bayt</a></i> (the "Family of the House", Muhammad's direct descendants).</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Before the advent of Islam, Arabian <a href="/wiki/Monarch" title="Monarch">monarchs</a> traditionally used the title <i><a href="/wiki/Malik" title="Malik">malik</a></i> (King, ruler), or another from the same <a href="/wiki/Semitic_root" title="Semitic root">root</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>The term <i>caliph</i> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/eɪ/ long 'a' in 'base'">eɪ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/ɪ/ short 'i' in 'bid'">ɪ</span><span title="'f' in 'find'">f</span></span>, <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/æ/ short 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/ɪ/ short 'i' in 'bid'">ɪ</span><span title="'f' in 'find'">f</span></span>/</a></span></span>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> derives from the <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> word <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="ar ALA transliteration">khalīfah</span></i> (<span lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">خَليفة</span>, <span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:Ar-khalifa.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a> </span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/67/Ar-khalifa.ogg" title="Ar-khalifa.ogg">pronunciation</a></span> <small class="metadata audiolinkinfo" style="cursor:help;">(<a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help"><span style="cursor:help;">help</span></a>·<a href="/wiki/File:Ar-khalifa.ogg" title="File:Ar-khalifa.ogg"><span style="cursor:help;">info</span></a>)</small></span>), which means "successor", "steward", or "deputy" and has traditionally been considered a shortening of either the term <i>khalifat Allah</i> ("successor to God") or <i>khalifat rasul Allah</i> ("successor of the messenger of God]"). However, studies of pre-Islamic texts suggest that the original meaning of the phrase was "successor <i>selected by</i> God."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-:0-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rashidun_Caliphate_.28632.E2.80.93661.29">Rashidun Caliphate (632–661)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Rashidun Caliphate (632–661)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Succession_to_Muhammad">Succession to Muhammad</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Succession to Muhammad">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Succession_to_Muhammad" title="Succession to Muhammad">Succession to Muhammad</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rashidun_Caliphate" title="Rashidun Caliphate">Rashidun Caliphate</a></div>
<p>In his book <i>The Early Islamic Conquests</i> (1981), <a href="/wiki/Fred_Donner" title="Fred Donner">Fred Donner</a> argues that the standard Arabian practice at the time was for the prominent men of a kinship group, or tribe, to gather after a leader's death and elect a leader from amongst themselves.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> There was no specified procedure for this <a href="/wiki/Shura" title="Shura">shura</a> or consultation. Candidates were usually, but not necessarily, from the same lineage as the deceased leader. Capable men who would lead well were preferred over an ineffectual heir.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam" title="Sunni Islam">Sunni Muslims</a> believe that <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr" title="Abu Bakr">Abu Bakr</a> was chosen by the community and that this was the proper procedure. Sunnis further argue that a caliph should ideally be chosen by election or community consensus.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam" title="Shia Islam">The Shia</a> believe that <a href="/wiki/Ali" title="Ali">Ali</a>, the son-in-law and cousin of Muhammad, was chosen by Muhammad as his spiritual and temporal successor as the <i>Mawla</i> (the Imam and the Caliph) of all Muslims in <a href="/wiki/The_event_of_Ghadir_Khumm" title="The event of Ghadir Khumm">the event of Ghadir Khumm</a>. Here Mohammad called upon the around 100,000 gathered returning pilgrims to give their <a href="/wiki/Bay%27ah" title="Bay'ah">bay'ah</a> (oath of allegiance) to Ali in his very presence and thenceforth to proclaim the good news of Ali's succession to his (Muhammad's) leadership to all Muslims they should come across.</p>
<p>The caliph was often known as <i><a href="/wiki/Amir_al-Mu%27minin" title="Amir al-Mu'minin">Amir al-Mu'minin</a></i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">أمير المؤمنين</span>‎‎ "Commander of the Believers"). <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> established his capital in <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medina</a>; after he died, it remained the capital during the Rashidun Caliphate, before <a href="/wiki/Kufa" title="Kufa">Kufa</a> was reportedly made the capital by Caliph Ali. At times there have been rival claimant caliphs in different parts of the Islamic world, and divisions between the Shi'i and Sunni communities.</p>
<p>According to Sunni Muslims, the first caliph to be called <i>Amir al-Mu'minin</i> was Abu Bakr, followed by <a href="/wiki/Umar" title="Umar">Umar</a>, the second of the <a href="/wiki/Rashidun" title="Rashidun">Rashidun</a>. <a href="/wiki/Uthman" title="Uthman">Uthman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ali" title="Ali">Ali</a> also were called by the same title, while the Shi'a consider Ali to have been the only truly legitimate caliph, of these four men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the first four caliphs, the Caliphate was claimed by the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyad Caliphate</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid Caliphate</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>, and for relatively short periods by other, competing dynasties in <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">al-Andalus</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>. After the collapse of the Ottoman Empire, <a href="/wiki/Mustafa_Kemal_Atat%C3%BCrk" title="Mustafa Kemal Atatürk">Mustafa Kemal Atatürk</a> officially abolished the system of Caliphate in Islam (the Ottoman Empire) as part of his <a href="/wiki/Atat%C3%BCrk%27s_Reforms" title="Atatürk's Reforms">secular reforms</a> and founded the <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Republic of Turkey</a> in 1923. The <a href="/wiki/List_of_rulers_of_Morocco" title="List of rulers of Morocco">Kings of Morocco</a> still label themselves with the title <i>Amir al-Mu'minin</i> for the <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Morocco" title="Demographics of Morocco">Moroccans</a>, but lay no claim to the Caliphate.</p>
<p>Some Muslim countries, including <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>, were never subject to the authority of a Caliphate<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Indonesia (especially Java) had historical relation with Ottoman Caliphate, Wali Sanga (The Nine Walis) was sent by Muhammad I of Ottoman, there was Quran in Malay language written in its cover 'Printed by Ottoman Sultan, King of all muslims'. (June 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>, with the exception of <a href="/wiki/Aceh" title="Aceh">Aceh</a>, which briefly acknowledged Ottoman suzerainty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Consequently, these countries had their own, local, sultans or rulers who did not fully accept the authority of the Caliph.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rashidun_Caliphs">Rashidun Caliphs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Rashidun Caliphs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Rashidun" title="Rashidun">Rashidun</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rashidun_Caliphate" title="Rashidun Caliphate">Rashidun Caliphate</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Early_Muslim_conquests" title="Early Muslim conquests">early Muslim conquests</a> and <a href="/wiki/Succession_to_Muhammad" title="Succession to Muhammad">Succession to Muhammad</a></div>

<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mohammad_adil-Rashidun-empire-at-its-peak-close.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="580" data-file-width="1196" height="107" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Mohammad_adil-Rashidun-empire-at-its-peak-close.PNG/220px-Mohammad_adil-Rashidun-empire-at-its-peak-close.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Mohammad_adil-Rashidun-empire-at-its-peak-close.PNG/330px-Mohammad_adil-Rashidun-empire-at-its-peak-close.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Mohammad_adil-Rashidun-empire-at-its-peak-close.PNG/440px-Mohammad_adil-Rashidun-empire-at-its-peak-close.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mohammad_adil-Rashidun-empire-at-its-peak-close.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Rashidun Caliphate at its greatest extent, under Caliph Uthman's rule</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Abu Bakr, the first successor of Muhammad, nominated Umar as his successor on his deathbed. Umar, the second caliph, was killed by a Persian named <a href="/wiki/Piruz_Nahavandi" title="Piruz Nahavandi">Piruz Nahavandi</a>. His successor, Uthman, was elected by a council of electors (<a href="/wiki/Majlis" title="Majlis">majlis</a>). Uthman was killed by members of a disaffected group. <a href="/wiki/Ali" title="Ali">Ali</a> then took control but was not universally accepted as caliph by the governors of Egypt, and later by some of his own guard. He faced two major rebellions and was assassinated by <a href="/wiki/Abd-al-Rahman_ibn_Muljam" title="Abd-al-Rahman ibn Muljam">Abd-al-Rahman ibn Muljam</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Khawarij" title="Khawarij">Khawarij</a>. Ali's tumultuous rule lasted only five years. This period is known as the <a href="/wiki/First_Fitna" title="First Fitna">Fitna</a>, or the first Islamic civil war. The followers of Ali later became the Shi'a ("shiaat Ali", partisans of Ali.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-econ1_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-econ1-7">[7]</a></sup>) minority sect of Islam and reject the legitimacy of the first 3 caliphs. The followers of all four Rashidun Caliphs (Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman and Ali) became the majority Sunni sect.</p>
<p>Under the Rashidun each region (<a href="/wiki/Sultan" title="Sultan">Sultanate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wilayah" title="Wilayah">Wilayah</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Emirate" title="Emirate">Emirate</a>) of the Caliphate had its own governor (Sultan, <a href="/wiki/Vali_(governor)" title="Vali (governor)">Wāli</a> or <a href="/wiki/Emir" title="Emir">Emir</a>). <a href="/wiki/Muawiyah_I" title="Muawiyah I">Muawiyah</a>, a relative of Uthman and governor (<i>Wali</i>) of <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, succeeded Ali as Caliph. Muawiyah transformed the caliphate into a <a href="/wiki/Inheritance" title="Inheritance">hereditary</a> office, thus founding the Umayyad <a href="/wiki/Dynasty" title="Dynasty">dynasty</a>.</p>
<p>In areas which were previously under <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sasanian Empire</a> or <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine</a> rule, the Caliphs lowered taxes, provided greater local autonomy (to their delegated governors), greater religious freedom for <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a>, and some indigenous <a href="/wiki/Christian" title="Christian">Christians</a>, and brought peace to peoples demoralized and disaffected by the casualties and heavy taxation that resulted from the decades of <a href="/wiki/Roman%E2%80%93Persian_Wars" title="Roman–Persian Wars">Byzantine-Persian warfare</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ali.27s_caliphate_and_the_rise_of_the_Umayyad_dynasty">Ali's caliphate and the rise of the Umayyad dynasty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Ali's caliphate and the rise of the Umayyad dynasty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Ali's reign was plagued by great turmoil and internal strife. The primary one came from a misunderstanding on the part of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mu%27awiyah" title="Mu'awiyah">Mu'awiyah</a>, the governor of <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a>. The Persians, taking advantage of this, infiltrated the two armies and attacked the other army causing chaos and internal hatred between the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Companions_of_Muhammad" title="Companions of Muhammad">companions</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Siffin" title="Battle of Siffin">Battle of Siffin</a>. The battle lasted several months, resulting in a stalemate. In order to avoid further bloodshed, Ali agreed to negotiate with Mu'awiyah. This caused a faction of approximately 4,000 people that would be known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kharijites" title="Kharijites">Kharijites</a>, to abandon the fight. After defeating the Kharijites at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Nahrawan" title="Battle of Nahrawan">Battle of Nahrawan</a>, Ali was later assassinated by the Kharijite Ibn Muljam. Ali's son <a href="/wiki/Hasan_ibn_Ali" title="Hasan ibn Ali">Hasan</a> was elected as the next caliph, but handed his title to Mu'awiyah a few months later. Mu'awiyah became the fifth (or second by Shia reckoning) caliph, establishing the Umayyad Dynasty,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> named after the great-grandfather of Uthman and Mu'awiyah, <a href="/wiki/Umayya_ibn_Abd_Shams" title="Umayya ibn Abd Shams">Umayya ibn Abd Shams</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Umayyad_Caliphate_.28661.E2.80.93750.29">Umayyad Caliphate (661–750)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Umayyad Caliphate (661–750)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyad Caliphate</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="413" data-file-width="900" height="101" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg/220px-Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg/330px-Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg/440px-Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_expansion_of_Caliphate.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Caliphate, 622–750
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #a1584e; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Expansion under Muhammad, 622–632</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ef9070; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Expansion during the Rashidun Caliphs, 632–661</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #fad07d; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Expansion during the Umayyad Caliphate, 661–750</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Under the Umayyads, the Caliphate grew rapidly in territory, incorporating the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transoxiana" title="Transoxiana">Transoxiana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sindh" title="Sindh">Sindh</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a> and most of the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a> (<a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Al-Andalus</a>) into the Muslim world. At its greatest extent, the Umayyad Caliphate covered 5.17 million square miles (13,400,000 km<sup>2</sup>), making it the <a href="/wiki/List_of_largest_empires" title="List of largest empires">largest empire</a> the world had yet seen, and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_largest_empires#All_empires_at_their_greatest_extent" title="List of largest empires">sixth-largest ever to exist</a> in history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blankinship_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blankinship-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Geographically, the empire was divided into several provinces, the borders of which changed numerous times during the Umayyad reign. Each province had a governor appointed by the caliph. However, for a variety of reasons, including that they were not elected by <a href="/wiki/Shura" title="Shura">Shura</a> and suggestions of impious behaviour, the Umayyad dynasty was not universally supported within the Muslim community. Some supported prominent early Muslims like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Zubayr" title="Al-Zubayr">Al-Zubayr</a>; others felt that only members of Muhammad's clan, the <a href="/wiki/Banu_Hashim" title="Banu Hashim">Banu Hashim</a>, or his own lineage, the descendants of Ali, should rule.</p>
<p>There were numerous rebellions against the Umayyads, as well as splits within the Umayyad ranks (notably, the rivalry between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qays_and_Yaman_tribes" title="Qays and Yaman tribes">Yaman and Qays</a>). Allegedly, Shimr Ibn Thil-Jawshan killed Ali's son <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hussein_ibn_Ali" title="Hussein ibn Ali">Hussein</a> and his family at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Karbala" title="Battle of Karbala">Battle of Karbala</a> in 680, solidifying the <a href="/wiki/Shia%E2%80%93Sunni_relations" title="Shia–Sunni relations">Shia-Sunni split</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-econ1_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-econ1-7">[7]</a></sup> Eventually, supporters of the Banu Hashim and the supporters of the lineage of Ali united to bring down the Umayyads in 750. However, the <i>Shi‘at ‘Alī</i>, "the Party of Ali", were again disappointed when the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abbasid_dynasty" title="Abbasid dynasty">Abbasid dynasty</a> took power, as the Abbasids were descended from Muhammad's uncle, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%E2%80%98Abbas_ibn_%E2%80%98Abd_al-Muttalib" title="‘Abbas ibn ‘Abd al-Muttalib">‘Abbas ibn ‘Abd al-Muttalib</a> and not from Ali.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Abbasid_Caliphate_.28750.E2.80.931258.2C_1261.E2.80.931517.29">Abbasid Caliphate (750–1258, 1261–1517)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Abbasid Caliphate (750–1258, 1261–1517)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid Caliphate</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Abbassid_Caliphs_at_Baghdad">Abbassid Caliphs at Baghdad</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Abbassid Caliphs at Baghdad">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mustansiriya_University_CPT.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2240" data-file-width="1680" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Mustansiriya_University_CPT.jpg/170px-Mustansiriya_University_CPT.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Mustansiriya_University_CPT.jpg/255px-Mustansiriya_University_CPT.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Mustansiriya_University_CPT.jpg/340px-Mustansiriya_University_CPT.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mustansiriya_University_CPT.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Al-Mustansiriya_University" title="Al-Mustansiriya University">Al-Mustansiriya University</a> in <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 750, the Umayyad dynasty was overthrown by another family of <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Meccan</a> origin, the Abbasids. Their time was marked by scientific, cultural and religious prosperity. Islamic art and music also flourished significantly during their reign. Their major city and capital <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a> began to flourish as a center of knowledge, culture and trade. This period of cultural fruition ended in 1258 with the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Baghdad_(1258)" title="Siege of Baghdad (1258)">sack of Baghdad</a> by the Mongols under <a href="/wiki/Hulagu_Khan" title="Hulagu Khan">Hulagu Khan</a>. The Abbasid Caliphate had however lost its effective power outside Iraq already by c. 920.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> By 945, the loss of power became official when the <a href="/wiki/Buyid_dynasty" title="Buyid dynasty">Buyids</a> conquered Baghdad and all of Iraq. The empire fell apart and its parts were ruled for the next century by local dynasties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 9th century, the Abbasids created an army loyal only to their caliphate, composed predominantly of Turkic Cuman, Circassian, and Georgian slave origin known as Mamluks. By 1250 the Mamluks came to power in Egypt. The Mamluk army, though often viewed negatively, both helped and hurt the caliphate. Early on, it provided the government with a stable force to address domestic and foreign problems. However, creation of this foreign army and al-Mu'tasim's transfer of the capital from Baghdad to Samarra created a division between the caliphate and the peoples they claimed to rule. In addition, the power of the Mamluks steadily grew until <a href="/wiki/Ar-Radi" title="Ar-Radi">Ar-Radi</a> (934–41) was constrained to hand over most of the royal functions to <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_ibn_Ra%27iq" title="Muhammad ibn Ra'iq">Muhammad ibn Ra'iq</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Under_the_Mamluk_Sultanate_of_Cairo_.281261.E2.80.931517.29">Under the Mamluk Sultanate of Cairo (1261–1517)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Under the Mamluk Sultanate of Cairo (1261–1517)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mamluk_Sultanate_(Cairo)" title="Mamluk Sultanate (Cairo)">Mamluk Sultanate (Cairo)</a></div>
<p>In 1261, following the devastation of Baghdad at the hands of the Mongols, the Mamluk rulers of Egypt tried to gain legitimacy for their rule by declaring the re-establishment of the Abbasid caliphate in <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a>. The Abbasid caliphs in Egypt had little to no political power; they continued to maintain the symbols of authority, but their sway was confined to religious matters. The first Abbasid caliph of Cairo was <a href="/wiki/Al-Mustansir_(Cairo)" title="Al-Mustansir (Cairo)">Al-Mustansir</a> (r. June–November 1261). The Abbasid caliphate of Cairo lasted until the time of <a href="/wiki/Al-Mutawakkil_III" title="Al-Mutawakkil III">Al-Mutawakkil III</a>, who ruled as caliph from 1508 to 1516, then he was deposed briefly in 1516 by his predecessor <a href="/wiki/Al-Mustamsik" title="Al-Mustamsik">Al-Mustamsik</a>, but was restored again to the caliphate in 1517.</p>
<p>The Ottoman Great Sultan <a href="/wiki/Selim_I" title="Selim I">Selim I</a> defeated the Mamluk Sultanate, and made Egypt part of the Ottoman Empire in 1517. Al-Mutawakkil III was captured together with his family and transported to Constantinople as a prisoner where he had a ceremonial role. He died in 1543, following his return to Cairo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Parallel_caliphates_to_the_Abbasids">Parallel caliphates to the Abbasids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Parallel caliphates to the Abbasids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Abbasid dynasty lost effective power over much of the Muslim realm by the first half of the tenth century.</p>
<p>The Shiʻa <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ubayd_Allah_al-Mahdi_Billah" title="Ubayd Allah al-Mahdi Billah">Ubayd Allah al-Mahdi Billah</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Fatimid_Caliphate" title="Fatimid Caliphate">Fatimid dynasty</a>, which claimed descent from Muhammad through his daughter, claimed the title of Caliph in 909, creating a separate line of caliphs in North Africa. Initially controlling <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a>, the Fatimid caliphs extended their rule for the next 150 years, taking Egypt and <a href="/wiki/Palestine_(region)" title="Palestine (region)">Palestine</a>, before the Abbasid dynasty was able to turn the tide, limiting Fatimid rule to Egypt. The Fatimid dynasty finally ended in 1171.</p>
<p>The Umayyad dynasty, which had survived and come to rule over <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Al-Andalus</a>, reclaimed the title of Caliph in 929, lasting until it was overthrown in 1031.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fatimid_Caliphate_.28909.E2.80.931171.29">Fatimid Caliphate (909–1171)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Fatimid Caliphate (909–1171)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fatimid_Caliphate" title="Fatimid Caliphate">Fatimid Caliphate</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fatimid_Caliphate.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="564" data-file-width="983" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Fatimid_Caliphate.PNG/220px-Fatimid_Caliphate.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Fatimid_Caliphate.PNG/330px-Fatimid_Caliphate.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Fatimid_Caliphate.PNG/440px-Fatimid_Caliphate.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fatimid_Caliphate.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map of the Fatimid Caliphate at its largest extent in the early 11th century</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Fatimid Caliphate was an <a href="/wiki/Isma%27ilism" title="Isma'ilism">Isma'ili Shi'i</a> caliphate that spanned a vast area of the <a href="/wiki/Arab_world" title="Arab world">Arab world</a>. Originally based in <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>, the Fatimid dynasty extended their rule across the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean coast</a> of <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, and ultimately made Egypt the centre of their caliphate. At its height, in addition to Egypt, the caliphate included varying areas of the <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Hejaz" title="Hejaz">Hejaz</a>.</p>
<p>The Fatimids established the Tunisian city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mahdia,_Tunisia" title="Mahdia, Tunisia">Mahdia</a> and made it their capital city, before conquering Egypt, and building the city of <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a> there in 969. Thereafter, Cairo became the capital of the caliphate, with Egypt becoming the political, cultural and religious centre of the state. Islam scholar <a href="/wiki/Louis_Massignon" title="Louis Massignon">Louis Massignon</a> dubbed the 4th century AH /10th century CE as the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ismailism" title="Ismailism">Ismaili</a> century in the history of Islam".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>The term <i>Fatimite</i> is sometimes used to refer to the citizens of this caliphate. The ruling elite of the state belonged to the Ismaili branch of Shi'ism. The leaders of the dynasty were <a href="/wiki/Imamah_(Ismaili_doctrine)" title="Imamah (Ismaili doctrine)">Ismaili Imams</a> and had a religious significance to Ismaili Muslims. They are also part of the chain of holders of the office of the Caliphate, as recognized by some Muslims. Therefore, this constitutes a rare period in history in which the descendants of Ali (hence the name Fatimid, referring to Ali's wife Fatima) and the Caliphate were united to any degree, excepting the final period of the <a href="/wiki/Rashidun_Caliphate" title="Rashidun Caliphate">Rashidun Caliphate</a> under <a href="/wiki/Ali" title="Ali">Ali</a> himself.</p>
<p>The caliphate was reputed to exercise a degree of religious tolerance towards non-Ismaili sects of Islam as well as towards <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maltese_people" title="Maltese people">Maltese Christians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Copts" title="Copts">Copts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Umayyad_Caliphate_of_C.C3.B3rdoba_.28929.E2.80.931031.29">Umayyad Caliphate of Córdoba (929–1031)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Umayyad Caliphate of Córdoba (929–1031)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_C%C3%B3rdoba" title="Emirate of Córdoba">Emirate of Córdoba</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caliphate_of_C%C3%B3rdoba" title="Caliphate of Córdoba">Caliphate of Córdoba</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Al-Andalus</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Califato_de_C%C3%B3rdoba_-_1000.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="594" data-file-width="686" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Califato_de_C%C3%B3rdoba_-_1000.svg/220px-Califato_de_C%C3%B3rdoba_-_1000.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Califato_de_C%C3%B3rdoba_-_1000.svg/330px-Califato_de_C%C3%B3rdoba_-_1000.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Califato_de_C%C3%B3rdoba_-_1000.svg/440px-Califato_de_C%C3%B3rdoba_-_1000.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Califato_de_C%C3%B3rdoba_-_1000.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map of the Caliphate of Cordoba c. 1000</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the Umayyad dynasty, the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a> was an integral province of the Umayyad Caliphate ruling from <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a>. The Umayyads lost the position of Caliph in Damascus in 750, and <a href="/wiki/Abd_al-Rahman_I" title="Abd al-Rahman I">Abd al-Rahman I</a> became Emir of Córdoba in 756 after six years in exile. Intent on regaining power, he defeated the existing Islamic rulers of the area who defied Umayyad rule and united various local fiefdoms into an emirate.</p>
<p>Rulers of the emirate used the title "emir" or "sultan" until the 10th century, when <a href="/wiki/Abd-ar-Rahman_III" title="Abd-ar-Rahman III">Abd al-Rahman III</a> was faced with the threat of invasion by the Fatimid Caliphate. To aid his fight against the invading Fatimids, who claimed the caliphate in opposition to the generally recognized Abbasid Caliph of Baghdad, <a href="/wiki/Al-Mu%27tadid" title="Al-Mu'tadid">Al-Mu'tadid</a>, Abd al-Rahman III claimed the title of caliph himself. This helped Abd al-Rahman III gain prestige with his subjects, and the title was retained after the Fatimids were repulsed. The rule of the Caliphate is considered as the heyday of Muslim presence in the Iberian peninsula, before it fragmented into various <a href="/wiki/Taifa" title="Taifa">taifas</a> in the 11th century. This period was characterized by a remarkable flourishing in technology, trade and culture; many of the masterpieces of <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">al-Andalus</a> were constructed in this period.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Almohad_Caliphate_.281147.E2.80.931269.29">Almohad Caliphate (1147–1269)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Almohad Caliphate (1147–1269)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Almohad_Caliphate" title="Almohad Caliphate">Almohad Caliphate</a></div>
<p>The Almohad Caliphate (<a href="/wiki/Berber_languages" title="Berber languages">Berber</a>: <span lang="ber" xml:lang="ber">Imweḥḥden</span>, from <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> <span lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">الموحدون</span> <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Arabic transliteration">al-Muwaḥḥidun</span></i>, "<a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">the Monotheists</a>" or "the Unifiers") was a Moroccan<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Berbers" title="Berbers">Berber</a> <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> movement founded in the 12th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brit1_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-brit1-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Almohad movement was started by <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Tumart" title="Ibn Tumart">Ibn Tumart</a> among the <a href="/wiki/Masmuda" title="Masmuda">Masmuda</a> tribes of southern Morocco. The Almohads first established a Berber state in <a href="/wiki/Tinmel" title="Tinmel">Tinmel</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Atlas_Mountains" title="Atlas Mountains">Atlas Mountains</a> in roughly 1120.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brit1_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-brit1-19">[19]</a></sup> The Almohads succeeded in overthrowing the <a href="/wiki/Almoravid_dynasty" title="Almoravid dynasty">Almoravid dynasty</a> in governing Morocco by 1147, when <a href="/wiki/Abd_al-Mu%27min" title="Abd al-Mu'min">Abd al-Mu'min</a> (r. 1130-1163) conquered Marrakech and declared himself Caliph. They then extended their power over all of the <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a> by 1159. Al-Andalus followed the fate of Africa and all Islamic Iberia was under Almohad rule by 1172.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Almohad dominance of Iberia continued until 1212, when <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_al-Nasir" title="Muhammad al-Nasir">Muhammad al-Nasir</a> (1199–1214) was defeated at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Las_Navas_de_Tolosa" title="Battle of Las Navas de Tolosa">Battle of Las Navas de Tolosa</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Morena" title="Sierra Morena">Sierra Morena</a> by an alliance of the Christian princes of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Castile" title="Kingdom of Castile">Castile</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_Aragon" title="Crown of Aragon">Aragon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Navarre" title="Kingdom of Navarre">Navarre</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Portugal" title="Kingdom of Portugal">Portugal</a>. Nearly all of the <a href="/wiki/Moors" title="Moors">Moorish</a> dominions in Iberia were lost soon after, with the great Moorish cities of <a href="/wiki/C%C3%B3rdoba,_Andalusia" title="Córdoba, Andalusia">Córdoba</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seville" title="Seville">Seville</a> falling to the Christians in 1236 and 1248, respectively.</p>
<p>The Almohads continued to rule in northern Africa until the piecemeal loss of territory through the revolt of tribes and districts enabled the rise of their most effective enemies, the <a href="/wiki/Marinid_dynasty" title="Marinid dynasty">Marinid dynasty</a>, in 1215. The last representative of the line, <a href="/wiki/Idris_al-Wathiq" title="Idris al-Wathiq">Idris al-Wathiq</a>, was reduced to the possession of <a href="/wiki/Marrakesh" title="Marrakesh">Marrakesh</a>, where he was murdered by a slave in 1269; the Marinids seized Marrakesh, ending the Almohad domination of the Western <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ottoman_Caliphate_.281517.E2.80.931924.29">Ottoman Caliphate (1517–1924)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Ottoman Caliphate (1517–1924)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Caliphate" title="Ottoman Caliphate">Ottoman Caliphate</a></div>
<p>The caliphate was claimed by the <a href="/wiki/List_of_sultans_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="List of sultans of the Ottoman Empire">sultans of the Ottoman Empire</a> beginning with <a href="/wiki/Murad_I" title="Murad I">Murad I</a> (reigned 1362 to 1389),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lambton_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lambton-21">[21]</a></sup> while recognizing no authority on the part of the Abbasid caliphs of the Mamluk-ruled Cairo. Hence the seat of the caliphate moved to the Ottoman capital of <a href="/wiki/Edirne" title="Edirne">Edirne</a>. In 1453, after <a href="/wiki/Mehmed_the_Conqueror" title="Mehmed the Conqueror">Mehmed the Conqueror</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">conquest of Constantinople</a>, the seat of the Ottomans moved to <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>, present-day <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a>. In 1517, the Ottoman sultan <a href="/wiki/Selim_I" title="Selim I">Selim I</a> defeated and annexed the Mamluk Sultanate of Cairo into his empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritCal_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-BritCal-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sourdel_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sourdel-23">[23]</a></sup> Through conquering and unifying Muslim lands, Selim I became the defender of the Holy Cities of <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a> and <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medina</a>, which further strengthened the Ottoman claim to the caliphate in the Muslim world. Ottomans gradually came to be viewed as the de facto leaders and representatives of the Islamic world. However, the earlier Ottoman caliphs did not officially bear the title of caliph in their documents of state, inscriptions, or coinage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sourdel_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sourdel-23">[23]</a></sup> It was only in the late eighteenth century that the claim to the caliphate was discovered by the sultans to have a practical use, since it allowed them to counter Russian claims to protect Ottoman Christians with their own claim to protect Muslims under Russian rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karpat_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Karpat-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to Barthold, the first time the title of "caliph" was used as a political instead of symbolic religious title by the Ottomans was the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_K%C3%BC%C3%A7%C3%BCk_Kaynarca" title="Treaty of Küçük Kaynarca">Treaty of Küçük Kaynarca</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a> in 1774, when the Empire retained <a href="/wiki/Moral_authority" title="Moral authority">moral authority</a> on territory whose sovereignty was ceded to the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a>.</p>
<p>The British supported and propagated the view that the Ottomans were Caliphs of Islam among Muslims in British India and the Ottoman Sultans helped the British by issuing pronouncements to the Muslims of India telling them to support British rule from Sultan Ali III and Sultan Abdülmecid I.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Qureshi1999_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Qureshi1999-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>The outcome of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russo-Turkish_War_(1768%E2%80%9374)" title="Russo-Turkish War (1768–74)">Russo-Turkish War of 1768–74</a> was disastrous for the Ottomans. Large territories, including those with large Muslim populations, such as <a href="/wiki/Crimea" title="Crimea">Crimea</a>, were lost to the Russian Empire. However, the Ottomans under <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Hamid_I" title="Abdul Hamid I">Abdul Hamid I</a> claimed a diplomatic victory by being allowed to remain the religious leaders of Muslims in the now-independent Crimea as part of the peace treaty; in return Russia became the official protector of Christians in Ottoman territory.</p>
<p>Around 1880 Sultan <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Hamid_II" title="Abdul Hamid II">Abdul Hamid II</a> reasserted the title as a way of countering Russian expansion into Muslim lands. His claim was most fervently accepted by the Muslims of <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">British India</a>. By the eve of <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, the Ottoman state, despite its weakness relative to Europe, represented the largest and most powerful independent Islamic political entity. The sultan also enjoyed some authority beyond the borders of his shrinking empire as caliph of Muslims in Egypt, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>.</p>
<p>In 1899 <a href="/wiki/John_Hay" title="John Hay">John Hay</a>, U.S. Secretary of State, asked the American ambassador to Ottoman Turkey, <a href="/wiki/Oscar_Straus_(politician)" title="Oscar Straus (politician)">Oscar Straus</a>, to approach Sultan Abdul Hamid II to use his position as caliph to order the <a href="/wiki/Taus%C5%ABg_people" title="Tausūg people">Tausūg people</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Sulu" title="Sultanate of Sulu">Sultanate of Sulu</a> in the Philippines to submit to American suzerainty and American military rule; the Sultan obliged them and wrote the letter which was sent to Sulu via Mecca. As a result, the "Sulu Mohammedans ... refused to join the insurrectionists and had placed themselves under the control of our army, thereby recognizing American sovereignty."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karpat2001_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Karpat2001-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karpat2001_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Karpat2001-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yegar2002_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yegar2002-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Abolition_of_the_Caliphate_.281924.29">Abolition of the Caliphate (1924)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Abolition of the Caliphate (1924)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Atat%C3%BCrk%27s_Reforms" title="Atatürk's Reforms">Atatürk's Reforms</a></div>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">

<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:264px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Portrait_Caliph_Abdulmecid_II.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1185" height="354" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Portrait_Caliph_Abdulmecid_II.jpg/262px-Portrait_Caliph_Abdulmecid_II.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Portrait_Caliph_Abdulmecid_II.jpg/393px-Portrait_Caliph_Abdulmecid_II.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Portrait_Caliph_Abdulmecid_II.jpg/524px-Portrait_Caliph_Abdulmecid_II.jpg 2x" width="262"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Portrait_Caliph_Abdulmecid_II.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Abd%C3%BClmecid_II" title="Abdülmecid II">Abdülmecid II</a> was the last Caliph of Islam from the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_dynasty" title="Ottoman dynasty">Ottoman dynasty</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After the <a href="/wiki/Armistice_of_Mudros" title="Armistice of Mudros">Armistice of Mudros</a> of October 1918 with the military <a href="/wiki/Occupation_of_Constantinople" title="Occupation of Constantinople">occupation of Constantinople</a> and <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Versailles" title="Treaty of Versailles">Treaty of Versailles</a> (1919), the position of the Ottomans was uncertain. The movement to protect or restore the Ottomans gained force after the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_S%C3%A8vres" title="Treaty of Sèvres">Treaty of Sèvres</a> (August 1920) which imposed the <a href="/wiki/Partitioning_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Partitioning of the Ottoman Empire">partitioning of the Ottoman Empire</a> and gave Greece a powerful position in Anatolia, to the distress of the Turks. They called for help and the movement was the result. The movement had collapsed by late 1922.</p>

<div class="NavContent hlist" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center">

<td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;">
<p><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_coloured_voting_box.svg"><img alt="A coloured voting box.svg" class="noviewer" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/16px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/24px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/01/A_coloured_voting_box.svg/32px-A_coloured_voting_box.svg.png 2x" width="16"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Politics" title="Portal:Politics">Politics portal</a></p>
<a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Allah-green.svg"><img alt="Allah-green.svg" class="noviewer" data-file-height="215" data-file-width="206" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Allah-green.svg/15px-Allah-green.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Allah-green.svg/23px-Allah-green.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Allah-green.svg/31px-Allah-green.svg.png 2x" width="15"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Islam" title="Portal:Islam">Islam portal</a></td>

<tr>
<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Islamism_sidebar" title="Template:Islamism sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Islamism_sidebar" title="Template talk:Islamism sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Islamism_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>On March 3, 1924, the first <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_Turkey" title="List of Presidents of Turkey">President of the Turkish Republic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mustafa_Kemal_Atat%C3%BCrk" title="Mustafa Kemal Atatürk">Mustafa Kemal Atatürk</a>, as part of <a href="/wiki/Atat%C3%BCrk%27s_Reforms" title="Atatürk's Reforms">Atatürk's Reforms</a>, constitutionally abolished the institution of the caliphate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritCal_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-BritCal-22">[22]</a></sup> Its powers within Turkey were transferred to the <a href="/wiki/Grand_National_Assembly_of_Turkey" title="Grand National Assembly of Turkey">Grand National Assembly of Turkey</a>, the parliament of the newly formed Turkish Republic. The title was then <a href="/wiki/Sharifian_Caliphate" title="Sharifian Caliphate">claimed</a> by <a href="/wiki/Hussein_bin_Ali,_Sharif_of_Mecca" title="Hussein bin Ali, Sharif of Mecca">Hussein bin Ali, Sharif of Mecca</a> of <a href="/wiki/Hejaz" title="Hejaz">Hejaz</a>, leader of the <a href="/wiki/Arab_Revolt" title="Arab Revolt">Arab Revolt</a>, but his kingdom was defeated and annexed by <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Saud" title="Ibn Saud">ibn Saud</a> in 1925.</p>
<p>A summit was convened at <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a> in 1926 to discuss the revival of the Caliphate, but most Muslim countries did not participate and no action was taken to implement the summit's resolutions.</p>
<p>Though the title <i>Ameer al-Mumineen</i> was adopted by the King of Morocco and by <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Omar" title="Mohammed Omar">Mohammed Omar</a>, former head of the <a href="/wiki/Taliban" title="Taliban">Taliban</a> of <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, neither claimed any legal standing or authority over Muslims outside the borders of their respective countries.</p>
<p>Since the end of the Ottoman Empire, occasional demonstrations have been held calling for the reestablishment of the Caliphate. Organisations which call for the re-establishment of the Caliphate include <a href="/wiki/Hizb_ut-Tahrir" title="Hizb ut-Tahrir">Hizb ut-Tahrir</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_Brotherhood" title="Muslim Brotherhood">Muslim Brotherhood</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sokoto_Caliphate_.281804.E2.80.931903.29">Sokoto Caliphate (1804–1903)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Sokoto Caliphate (1804–1903)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sokoto_Caliphate" title="Sokoto Caliphate">Sokoto Caliphate</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Sokoto_Caliphate" title="Sokoto Caliphate">Sokoto Caliphate</a> was an Islamic state in what is now <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Usman_dan_Fodio" title="Usman dan Fodio">Usman dan Fodio</a>. Founded during the <a href="/wiki/Fulani_War" title="Fulani War">Fulani War</a> in the early 19th century, it controlled one of the most powerful empires in <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a> prior to European conquest and colonization. The caliphate remained extant through the colonial period and afterwards, though with reduced power.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The current head of the Sokoto Caliphate is <a href="/wiki/Sa%27adu_Abubakar" title="Sa'adu Abubakar">Sa'adu Abubakar</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Khilafat_Movement_.281919.E2.80.9324.29">Khilafat Movement (1919–24)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Khilafat Movement (1919–24)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Khilafat_Movement" title="Khilafat Movement">Khilafat Movement</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Partitioning_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Partitioning of the Ottoman Empire">Partitioning of the Ottoman Empire</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Khilafat_Movement" title="Khilafat Movement">Khilafat Movement</a> was launched by Muslims in <a href="/wiki/Presidencies_and_provinces_of_British_India" title="Presidencies and provinces of British India">British India</a> to defend the Ottoman Caliphate <a href="/wiki/Aftermath_of_World_War_I" title="Aftermath of World War I">at the end of the First World War</a> and it spread throughout the British colonial territories. It was strong in British India where it formed a rallying point for some Indian Muslims as one of many anti-British Indian political movements. Its leaders included <a href="/wiki/Mohammad_Ali_Jouhar" title="Mohammad Ali Jouhar">Mohammad Ali Jouhar</a>, his brother Shawkat Ali, and <a href="/wiki/Abul_Kalam_Azad" title="Abul Kalam Azad">Abul Kalam Azad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mukhtar_Ahmed_Ansari" title="Mukhtar Ahmed Ansari">Mukhtar Ahmed Ansari</a>, and Barrister Muhammad Jan Abbasi. For a time it was supported by <a href="/wiki/Mahatma_Gandhi" title="Mahatma Gandhi">Mohandas Karamchand Gandhi</a>, who was a member of the Central Khilafat Committee.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> However, the movement lost its momentum after the arrest or flight of its leaders, and a series of offshoots splintered off from the main organization.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sharifian_Caliphate_.281924.E2.80.9325.29">Sharifian Caliphate (1924–25)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Sharifian Caliphate (1924–25)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sharifian_Caliphate" title="Sharifian Caliphate">Sharifian Caliphate</a></div>
<p>The <b>Sharifian Caliphate</b> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar" xml:lang="ar"><big>خلافة شريفية</big></span>‎‎) was an Arab caliphate proclaimed by the <a href="/wiki/Sharif_of_Mecca" title="Sharif of Mecca">Sharifian rulers</a> of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hejaz" title="Kingdom of Hejaz">Hejaz</a> in 1924, in lieu of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Caliphate" title="Ottoman Caliphate">Ottoman Caliphate</a>. The idea of the Sharifian Caliphate had been floating around since at least the 15th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Toward the end of the 19th century, it started to gain importance due to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decline_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Decline of the Ottoman Empire">decline</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>, which was heavily defeated in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russo-Turkish_War_(1877%E2%80%9378)" title="Russo-Turkish War (1877–78)">Russo-Turkish War of 1877–78</a>. There is little evidence, however, that the idea of a Sharifian Caliphate ever gained wide grassroots support in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_World" title="Muslim World">anywhere</a> else for that matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-political_caliphates">Non-political caliphates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Non-political caliphates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Though non-political, some <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufi</a> <a href="/wiki/Tariqa" title="Tariqa">orders</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khalifatul_Masih" title="Khalifatul Masih">Ahmadiyya movement</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> define themselves as caliphates. Their leaders are thus commonly referred to as <i>khalifas</i> (caliphs).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sufi_caliphates">Sufi caliphates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Sufi caliphates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tariqa" title="Tariqa">tariqas</a> (orders) are led by spiritual leaders (khilafah ruhaniyyah), the main khalifas, who nominate local khalifas to organize <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zaouia" title="Zaouia">zaouias</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sufi caliphates are not necessarily hereditary. Khalifas are aimed to serve the <i>silsilah</i> in relation to spiritual responsibilities and to propagate the teachings of the tariqa.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ahmadiyya_Caliphate_.281908-present.29">Ahmadiyya Caliphate (1908-present)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Ahmadiyya Caliphate (1908-present)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Liwa-e-Ahmadiyya_1-2.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="550" data-file-width="1100" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Liwa-e-Ahmadiyya_1-2.svg/300px-Liwa-e-Ahmadiyya_1-2.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Liwa-e-Ahmadiyya_1-2.svg/450px-Liwa-e-Ahmadiyya_1-2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Liwa-e-Ahmadiyya_1-2.svg/600px-Liwa-e-Ahmadiyya_1-2.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Liwa-e-Ahmadiyya_1-2.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Black_Standard#Ahmadiyya_flag" title="Black Standard">Ahmadiyya flag</a>, first designed in 1939, during the leadership of the <a href="/wiki/Mirza_Basheer-ud-Din_Mahmood_Ahmad" title="Mirza Basheer-ud-Din Mahmood Ahmad">Second Caliph</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ahmadiyya_Caliphate" title="Ahmadiyya Caliphate">Ahmadiyya Caliphate</a></div>
<p>The Ahmadiyya Muslim Community is an Islamic revivalist movement founded in 1889 by <a href="/wiki/Mirza_Ghulam_Ahmad" title="Mirza Ghulam Ahmad">Mirza Ghulam Ahmad</a> of <a href="/wiki/Qadian" title="Qadian">Qadian</a>, India, who claimed to be the promised Messiah and <a href="/wiki/Mahdi" title="Mahdi">Mahdi</a>, awaited by Muslims. He also claimed to be a follower-<a href="/wiki/Prophet" title="Prophet">prophet</a> subordinate to Muhammad the prophet of Islam. After his death in 1908, his first successor, <a href="/wiki/Hakeem_Noor-ud-Din" title="Hakeem Noor-ud-Din">Hakeem Noor-ud-Din</a> became the caliph of the community and assumed the title of <i>Khalifatul Masih</i> (Successor or Caliph of the Messiah).</p>
<p>Ahmadi Muslims believe that the Ahmadiyya Caliphate established after the passing of the community's founder is the re-establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Rashidun_Caliphate" title="Rashidun Caliphate">Rashidun Caliphate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The Ahmadiyya caliphate has spanned over a century, seen five caliphs and continues to operate under this structure, with the caliph having overall authority for all religious and organizational matters. According to Ahmadiyya thought, it is not essential for a caliph to be the head of a state, rather the religious and organisational significance of the caliphate is emphasised. It is above all a religious office, with the purpose to uphold, strengthen and spread Islam and maintain the high moral standards within the Muslim community established by <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>, who was not merely a political leader but primarily a religious leader. The caliphate is understood as a system dealing with the organisation of believers and relating to the administration (<i>nizām</i>) of the Muslim community whether or not it involves a governmental role.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alislam.org_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-alislam.org-37">[37]</a></sup> Being based on the 'precepts of Prophethood', the institution of caliphate can therefore, like prophethood, exist and flourish without a state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-38">[38]</a></sup> If a caliph does happen to bear governmental authority as a head of state, it is incidental and subsidiary in relation to his overall function as caliph which is applicable to believers transnationally and not limited to one particular state or political entity. The system of caliphate in Islam, thus understood, transcends national sovereignty and ethnic divide, forming a universal supra-national entity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alislam.org_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-alislam.org-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-38">[38]</a></sup> According to Ahmadi Muslims, the caliphate seeks to establish God's authority on earth and the caliph strives to uphold that authority within the community of followers. It is required that the caliph carry out his duties through consultation and taking into consideration the views of the members of the <a href="/wiki/Ahmadiyya#The_Consultative_Council" title="Ahmadiyya"><i>Majlis-ash-Shura</i></a> (consultative body). However, it is not incumbent upon him to always accept the views and recommendations of the members. The caliph has overall authority for all religious and organisational matters and is bound to decide and act in accordance with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qur%27an" title="Qur'an">Qur'an</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sunnah" title="Sunnah">sunnah</a>.</p>
<p>After <a href="/wiki/Hakeem_Noor-ud-Din" title="Hakeem Noor-ud-Din">Hakeem Noor-ud-Din</a>, the first caliph, the title of the Ahmadiyya caliph continued under <a href="/wiki/Mirza_Basheer-ud-Din_Mahmood_Ahmad" title="Mirza Basheer-ud-Din Mahmood Ahmad">Mirza Mahmud Ahmad</a>, who led the community for over 50 years. Following him were <a href="/wiki/Mirza_Nasir_Ahmad" title="Mirza Nasir Ahmad">Mirza Nasir Ahmad</a> and then <a href="/wiki/Mirza_Tahir_Ahmad" title="Mirza Tahir Ahmad">Mirza Tahir Ahmad</a> who were the third and fourth caliphs respectively. The current caliph is <a href="/wiki/Mirza_Masroor_Ahmad" title="Mirza Masroor Ahmad">Mirza Masroor Ahmad</a>, who lives in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> with a following of 10 to 20 million in over 200 countries and territories of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_basis">Religious basis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Religious basis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Political_aspects_of_Islam" title="Political aspects of Islam">Political aspects of Islam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Divisions_of_the_world_in_Islam" title="Divisions of the world in Islam">Divisions of the world in Islam</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Qur.27an">Qur'an</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Qur'an">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a> uses the term <i>khalifa</i> twice. First, in <a href="/wiki/Al-Baqara" title="Al-Baqara">al-Baqara</a>, 30, it refers to <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">God</a> creating humanity as his <i>khalifa</i> on Earth. Second, in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sad_(sura)" title="Sad (sura)">Sad</a>, 26, it addresses King <a href="/wiki/David_in_Islam" title="David in Islam">David</a> as God's <i>khalifa</i> and reminds him of his obligation to rule with justice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
