<p>In addition, the following excerpt from the Quran, known as the 'Istikhlaf Verse', is used by some to argue for a Quranic basis for Caliphate:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>God has promised those of you who have attained to faith and do righteous deeds that, of a certainty, He will make them <i>Khulifa</i> on earth, even as He caused [some of] those who lived before them to become <i>Khulifa</i>; and that, of a certainty, He will firmly establish for them the religion which He has been pleased to bestow on them; and that, of a certainty, He will cause their erstwhile state of fear to be replaced by a sense of security [seeing that] they worship Me [alone], not ascribing divine powers to aught beside Me. But all who, after [having understood] this, choose to deny the truth – it is they, they who are truly iniquitous!" (<a href="/wiki/An-Nur" title="An-Nur">An-Nur</a>, 55)</p>
</blockquote>
<p>In the above verse, the word <i>Khulifa</i> (the plural of <i>Khalifa</i>) has been variously translated as "successors" and "ones who accede to power".</p>
<p>Small subsections of Sunni Islamism argue that to govern a state by <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Sharia</a> is, by definition, to rule via the Caliphate, and use the following verses to sustain their claim.</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>So govern between the people by that which God has revealed (Islam), and follow not their vain desires, beware of them in case they seduce you from just some part of that which God has revealed to you</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— <sup>[<a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a> <a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/005:049" rel="nofollow">005:049</a>]</sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>O you who believe! Obey God, and obey the messenger and then those among you who are in authority; and if you have a dispute concerning any matter, refer it to God and the messenger's rulings, if you are (in truth) believers in God and the Last Day. That is better and more seemly in the end.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— <sup>[<a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a> <a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/004:059" rel="nofollow">004:059</a>]</sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hadith"><i>Hadith</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Hadith">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The following <i><a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">hadith</a></i> from <a href="/wiki/Musnad_Ahmad_ibn_Hanbal" title="Musnad Ahmad ibn Hanbal">Musnad Ahmad ibn Hanbal</a> can be understood to prophesy two eras of Caliphate (both on the lines/precepts of prophethood).</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Hadhrat Huzaifa narrated that the Messenger of Allah said: Prophethood will remain among you as long as Allah wills. Then Caliphate (Khilafah) on the lines of Prophethood shall commence, and remain as long as Allah wills. Then corrupt/erosive monarchy would take place, and it will remain as long as Allah wills. After that, despotic kingship would emerge, and it will remain as long as Allah wills. Then, the Caliphate (Khilafah) shall come once again based on the precept of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prophethood" title="Prophethood">Prophethood</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (August 2010)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>In the above, the first era of Caliphate is commonly accepted by Muslims to be that of the <a href="/wiki/Rashidun" title="Rashidun">Rashidun</a> Caliphate.</p>
<p>Nafi'a reported saying:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>It has been reported on the authority of Nafi, that 'Abdullah b. Umar paid a visit to Abdullah b. Muti' in the days (when atrocities were perpetrated on the People Of Medina) at Harra in the time of Yazid b. Mu'awiya. Ibn Muti' said: Place a pillow for Abu 'Abd al-Rahman (family name of 'Abdullah b. 'Umar). But the latter said: I have not come to sit with you. I have come to you to tell you a tradition I heard from the Messenger of Allah. I heard him say: One who withdraws his band from obedience (to the Amir) will find no argument (in his defence) when he stands before Allah on the Day of Judgment, and one who dies without having bound himself by an oath of allegiance (to an Amir) will die the death of one belonging to the days of Jahiliyyah. – Sahih Muslim, Book 020, Hadith 4562.</p>
</blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hisham_ibn_Urwah" title="Hisham ibn Urwah">Hisham ibn Urwah</a> reported on the authority of Abu Saleh on the authority of <a href="/wiki/Abu_Hurairah" title="Abu Hurairah">Abu Hurairah</a> that Muhammad said:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Leaders will take charge of you after me, where the pious (one) will lead you with his piety and the impious (one) with his impiety, so only listen to them and obey them in everything which conforms with the truth (Islam). If they act rightly it is for your credit, and if they acted wrongly it is counted for you and against them.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Muslim narrated on the authority of al-A'araj, on the authority of <a href="/wiki/Abu_Hurairah" title="Abu Hurairah">Abu Hurairah</a>, that Muhammad said:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Behold, the Imam (Caliph) is but a shield from behind whom the people fight and by whom they defend themselves.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Muslim reported on the authority of <a href="/wiki/Abdel_Aziz_al-Muqrin" title="Abdel Aziz al-Muqrin">Abdel Aziz al-Muqrin</a>, who said:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>I accompanied Abu Hurairah for five years and heard him talking of Muhammd's saying: The Prophets ruled over the children of <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, whenever a Prophet died another Prophet succeeded him, but there will be no Prophet after me. There will be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caliph" title="Caliph">Khalifahs</a> and they will number many. They asked: What then do you order us? He said: Fulfil the bay'ah(transaction/sale) to them one after the other and give them their due. Surely God will ask them about what He entrusted them with.</p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prophesied_Caliphate_of_the_Mahd.C3.AE">Prophesied Caliphate of the Mahdî</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Prophesied Caliphate of the Mahdî">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>For information about Caliph the Mahdî, see "<a href="/wiki/Mahdi" title="Mahdi">Mahdi</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Islamic_eschatology" title="Islamic eschatology">Islamic eschatology</a>" (Section <a href="/wiki/Islamic_eschatology#Major_figures" title="Islamic eschatology">Islamic eschatology#Major figures</a>), and "<a href="/wiki/Second_Coming" title="Second Coming">Second Coming</a>" (Section <a href="/wiki/Second_Coming#Islam" title="Second Coming">Second Coming#Islam</a>).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Sahaba_of_Muhammad">The <i>Sahaba</i> of Muhammad</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: The Sahaba of Muhammad">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Al-Habbab Ibn ul-Munthir said, when the Sahaba met in the wake of the death of Muhammad, (at the <a href="/wiki/Saqifah" title="Saqifah">thaqifa</a> hall) of Bani Sa’ida:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Let there be one Amir from us and one Amir from you (meaning one from the Ansar and one from the Mohajireen).</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Upon this Abu Bakr replied:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>It is forbidden for Muslims to have two Amirs (rulers)...</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Then he got up and addressed the Muslims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (August 2010)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>It has additionally been reported<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> that Abu Bakr went on to say on the day of Al-Saqifa:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>It is forbidden for Muslims to have two Amirs for this would cause differences in their affairs and concepts, their unity would be divided and disputes would break out amongst them. The <a href="/wiki/Sunnah" title="Sunnah">Sunnah</a> would then be abandoned, the <a href="/wiki/Bid%E2%80%98ah" title="Bid‘ah">bida’a</a> (innovations) would spread and Fitna would grow, and that is in no one’s interests.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>The Sahaba agreed to this and selected Abu Bakr as their first Khaleef. Habbab ibn Mundhir who suggested the idea of two Ameers corrected himself and was the first to give Abu Bakr the <a href="/wiki/Bay%27ah" title="Bay'ah">Bay'ah</a>. This indicates an Ijma as-Sahaba of all of the Sahaba. Ali ibni abi Talib, who was attending the body of Muhammad at the time, also consented to this.</p>
<p>Imam Ali whom the Shia revere said:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>People must have an Amir...where the believer works under his Imara (rule) and under which the unbeliever would also benefit, until his rule ended by the end of his life (<i>ajal</i>), the booty (<i>fay’i</i>) would be gathered, the enemy would be fought, the routes would be made safe, the strong one will return what he took from the weak till the tyrant would be contained, and not bother anyone.</p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sayings_of_Islamic_theologians">Sayings of Islamic theologians</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Sayings of Islamic theologians">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Al-Mawardi" title="Al-Mawardi">Al-Mawardi</a> says:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>It is forbidden for the Ummah (Muslim world) to have two leaders at the same time.</p>
</blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Al-Nawawi" title="Al-Nawawi">Al-Nawawi</a> says:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>It is forbidden to give an oath to two leaders or more, even in different parts of the world and even if they are far apart.</p>
</blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ahmad_al-Qalqashandi" title="Ahmad al-Qalqashandi">Ahmad al-Qalqashandi</a> says:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>It is forbidden to appoint two leaders at the same time.</p>
</blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ibn_Hazm" title="Ibn Hazm">Ibn Hazm</a> says:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>It is permitted to have only one leader (of the Muslims) in the whole of the world.</p>
</blockquote>
<p><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Al-sha%E2%80%99rani&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Al-sha’rani (page does not exist)">Al-sha’rani</a> says:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>It is forbidden for Muslims to have in the whole world and at the same time two leaders whether in agreement or discord.</p>
</blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Abd_al-Jabbar_ibn_Ahmad" title="Abd al-Jabbar ibn Ahmad">Abd al-Jabbar ibn Ahmad</a> (a Mu’tazela scholar), says:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>It is forbidden to give the oath to more than one.</p>
</blockquote>
<p><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Al-Joziri&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Al-Joziri (page does not exist)">Al-Joziri</a> says:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The Imams (scholars of the four schools of thought)- may Allah have mercy on them- agree that the Caliphate is an obligation, and that the Muslims must appoint a leader who would implement the injunctions of the religion, and give the oppressed justice against the oppressors. It is forbidden for Muslims to have two leaders in the world whether in agreement or discord.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>The Shia schools of thought and others expressed the same opinion about this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> However, the Shia school of thought believe that the leader (Imam) must not be appointed by the Islamic ummah, but must be appointed by God.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Al-Qurtubi" title="Al-Qurtubi">Al-Qurtubi</a> said in his <a href="/wiki/Tafsir" title="Tafsir">Tafsir</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> of the verse, "Indeed, man is made upon this earth a Caliph"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> that:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>This Ayah is a source in the selection of an Imaam, and a Khaleef, he is listened to and he is obeyed, for the word is united through him, and the Ahkam (laws) of the Caliph are implemented through him, and there is no difference regarding the obligation of that between the Ummah, nor between the Imams except what is narrated about al-Asam, the Mu'tazzili ...</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Al-Qurtubi also said:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The Khilafah is the pillar upon which other pillars rest</p>
</blockquote>
<p>An-Nawawi said:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>(The scholars) consented that it is an obligation upon the Muslims to select a Khalif</p>
</blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Al-Ghazali" title="Al-Ghazali">Al-Ghazali</a> when writing of the potential consequences of losing the Caliphate said:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The judges will be suspended, the Wilayaat (provinces) will be nullified, ... the decrees of those in authority will not be executed and all the people will be on the verge of Haraam</p>
</blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ibn_Taymiyyah" title="Ibn Taymiyyah">Ibn Taymiyyah</a> said<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (August 2010)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup>:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>It is obligatory to know that the office in charge of commanding over the people (ie: the post of the Khaleefah) is one of the greatest obligations of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deen_(Arabic_term)" title="Deen (Arabic term)">Deen</a>. In fact, there is no establishment of the Deen except by it....this is the opinion of the <a href="/wiki/Salaf" title="Salaf">salaf</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Al-Fu%E1%B8%8Dayl_ibn_%E2%80%98Iy%C4%81%E1%B8%8D" title="Al-Fuḍayl ibn ‘Iyāḍ">Al-Fuḍayl ibn ‘Iyāḍ</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_ibn_Hanbal" title="Ahmad ibn Hanbal">Ahmad ibn Hanbal</a> and others</p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Period_of_dormancy">Period of dormancy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Period of dormancy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pan-Islamism" title="Pan-Islamism">Pan-Islamism</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Islamism" title="Islamism">Islamism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islamic_revival" title="Islamic revival">Islamic revival</a></div>
<p>Once the subject of intense conflict and rivalry amongst Muslim rulers, the caliphate laid dormant and largely unclaimed since the 1920s. For the vast majority of Muslims the caliph as leader of the <a href="/wiki/Ummah" title="Ummah">ummah</a>, "is cherished both as memory and ideal"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reun_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-reun-67">[67]</a></sup> as a time when Muslims "enjoyed scientific and military superiority globally".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> The Islamic prophet <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> is reported to have prophesied:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Prophethood will remain with you for as long as Allah wills it to remain, then Allah will raise it up whenever he wills to raise it up. Afterwards, there will be a Caliphate that follows the guidance of Prophethood remaining with you for as long as Allah wills it to remain. Then, He will raise it up whenever He wills to raise it up. Afterwards, there will be a reign of violently oppressive rule and it will remain with you for as long as Allah wills it to remain. Then, there will be a reign of tyrannical rule and it will remain for as long as Allah wills it to remain. Then, Allah will raise it up whenever He wills to raise it up. Then, there will be a Caliphate that follows the guidance of Prophethood.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— As-Silsilah As-Sahihah, vol. 1, no. 5</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islamic_State_of_Iraq_and_the_Levant_.282014.E2.80.93present.29">Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant (2014–present)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant (2014–present)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_State_of_Iraq_and_the_Levant" title="Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant">Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant</a></div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:408px;max-width:408px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Territorial_control_of_the_ISIS.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1264" data-file-width="1654" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Territorial_control_of_the_ISIS.svg/200px-Territorial_control_of_the_ISIS.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Territorial_control_of_the_ISIS.svg/300px-Territorial_control_of_the_ISIS.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Territorial_control_of_the_ISIS.svg/400px-Territorial_control_of_the_ISIS.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Syrian,_Iraqi,_and_Lebanese_insurgencies.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="3439" data-file-width="4501" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Syrian%2C_Iraqi%2C_and_Lebanese_insurgencies.png/200px-Syrian%2C_Iraqi%2C_and_Lebanese_insurgencies.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Syrian%2C_Iraqi%2C_and_Lebanese_insurgencies.png/300px-Syrian%2C_Iraqi%2C_and_Lebanese_insurgencies.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Syrian%2C_Iraqi%2C_and_Lebanese_insurgencies.png/400px-Syrian%2C_Iraqi%2C_and_Lebanese_insurgencies.png 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">ISIL's claimed Caliphate at its extent in May 2015 (left), and current situation, with ISIL's Caliphate in grey, as of November 16, 2016 (right)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The group <a href="/wiki/Tanzim_Qaidat_al-Jihad_fi_Bilad_al-Rafidayn" title="Tanzim Qaidat al-Jihad fi Bilad al-Rafidayn">Tanzim Qaidat al-Jihad fi Bilad al-Rafidayn</a> formed as an affiliate of <a href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda" title="Al-Qaeda">Al-Qaeda</a> network of <a href="/wiki/Islamism" title="Islamism">Islamist</a> militants during the <a href="/wiki/Iraq_War" title="Iraq War">Iraq War</a>. The group eventually expanded into <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> and rose to prominence as the Islamic State of Iraq and the Levant (ISIL) during the <a href="/wiki/Syrian_Civil_War" title="Syrian Civil War">Syrian Civil War</a>. In the summer of 2014, the group launched the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Iraq_offensive_(June_2014)" title="Northern Iraq offensive (June 2014)">Northern Iraq offensive</a>, seizing the city of <a href="/wiki/Mosul" title="Mosul">Mosul</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> The group declared itself a caliphate under <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr_al-Baghdadi" title="Abu Bakr al-Baghdadi">Abu Bakr al-Baghdadi</a> on June 29, 2014 and renamed itself as the "Islamic State".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>ISIL's claim to be the highest authority of Muslims has not been widely recognized beyond the territory it controls with 10 million people,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> and the group has been at war with armed forces including the <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Army" title="Iraqi Army">Iraqi Army</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Syrian_Army" title="Syrian Army">Syrian Army</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Free_Syrian_Army" title="Free Syrian Army">Free Syrian Army</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Al-Nusra_Front" title="Al-Nusra Front">Al-Nusra Front</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syrian_Democratic_Forces" title="Syrian Democratic Forces">Syrian Democratic Forces</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Iraqi_Kurdistan" title="Iraqi Kurdistan">Iraqi Kurdistan</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Peshmerga" title="Peshmerga">Peshmerga</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Protection_Units" title="People's Protection Units">People's Protection Units</a> (YPG) along with a 60 nation coalition in its efforts to establish a <i>de facto</i> state on Iraqi and Syrian territory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ahmadiyya_view">Ahmadiyya view</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Ahmadiyya view">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khalifatul_Masih" title="Khalifatul Masih">Khalifatul Masih</a></div>
<p>The members of the <a href="/wiki/Ahmadiyya" title="Ahmadiyya">Ahmadiyya community</a> believe that the Ahmadiyya Caliphate (Arabic: <i>Khilāfah</i>) is the continuation of the Islamic Caliphate, first being the <i>Rāshidūn</i> (rightly guided) Caliphate (of Righteous Caliphs). This is believed to have been suspended with Ali, the son-in-law of Muhammad and re-established with the appearance of <a href="/wiki/Mirza_Ghulam_Ahmad" title="Mirza Ghulam Ahmad">Mirza Ghulam Ahmad</a> (1835-1908, the founder of the movement) whom Ahmadis identify as the Promised Messiah and <a href="/wiki/Mahdi" title="Mahdi">Mahdi</a>.</p>
<p>Ahmadis maintain that in accordance with Quranic verses (such as <sup>[<a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a> <a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/24:55" rel="nofollow">24:55</a>]</sup>) and numerous ahadith on the issue, <i>Khilāfah</i> can only be established by God Himself and is a divine blessing given to <i>those who believe and work righteousness</i> and uphold the unity of God, therefore any movement to establish the <i>Khilāfah</i> centered on human endeavours alone is bound to fail, particularly when the condition of the people diverges from the ‘precepts of prophethood’ and they are as a result disunited, their inability to establish a <i>Khilāfah</i> caused fundamentally by the lack of righteousness in them. Although the khalifa is elected it is believed that God himself directs the hearts of believers towards an individual. Thus the khalifa is designated neither necessarily by right (i.e. the rightful or competent one in the eyes of the people at that time) nor merely by election but primarily by God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to Ahmadiyya thought, a khalifa need not be the head of a state; rather the Ahmadiyya community emphasises the spiritual and organisational significance of the Khilāfah. It is primarily a religious/spiritual office, with the purpose of upholding, strengthening and spreading Islam and of maintaining the high spiritual and moral standards within the global community established by <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a> - who was not merely a political leader but primarily a religious leader. If a khalifa does happen to bear governmental authority as a head of state, it is incidental and subsidiary in relation to his overall function as khalifa which is applicable to believers transnationally and not limited to one particular state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ahmadi Muslims believe that God has assured them that this Caliphate will endure to the end of time, depending on their righteousness and faith in God. The Khalifa provides unity, security, moral direction and progress for the community. It is required that the Khalifa carry out his duties through consultation and taking into consideration the views of the members of the <i>Shura</i> (consultative body). However, it is not incumbent upon him to always accept the views and recommendations of the members. The Khalifatul Masih has overall authority for all religious and organisational matters and is bound to decide and act in accordance with the Qur'an and sunnah.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islamic_call">Islamic call</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Islamic call">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A number of Islamist political parties and <a href="/wiki/Mujahideen" title="Mujahideen">mujahideen</a> called for the restoration of the caliphate by uniting Muslim nations, either through political action (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Hizb_ut-Tahrir" title="Hizb ut-Tahrir">Hizb ut-Tahrir</a>), or through force (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda" title="Al-Qaeda">al-Qaeda</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Various Islamist movements gained momentum in recent years with the ultimate aim of establishing a Caliphate. In 2014, ISIL/ISIS made a claim to re-establishing the Caliphate. Those advocating the re-establishment of a Caliphate differed in their methodology and approach. Some<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (November 2010)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> were locally oriented, mainstream political parties that had no apparent transnational objectives.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Abul_A%27la_Maududi" title="Abul A'la Maududi">Abul A'la Maududi</a> believed the caliph was not just an individual ruler who had to be restored, but was man's representation of <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">God</a>'s authority on Earth:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p><i>Khilafa</i> means representative. Man, according to Islam is the representative of "people", His (God's) viceregent; that is to say, by virtue of the powers delegated to him, and within the limits prescribed by the Qu'ran and the teaching of the prophet, the caliph is required to exercise Divine authority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Muslim_Brotherhood" title="Muslim Brotherhood">Muslim Brotherhood</a> advocates <a href="/wiki/Pan-Islamism" title="Pan-Islamism">pan-Islamic</a> unity and the implementation of <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Islamic law</a>. Founder <a href="/wiki/Hassan_al-Banna" title="Hassan al-Banna">Hassan al-Banna</a> wrote about the restoration of the Caliphate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>One transnational group whose ideology was based specifically on restoring the caliphate as a pan-Islamic state is <a href="/wiki/Hizb_ut-Tahrir" title="Hizb ut-Tahrir">Hizb ut-Tahrir</a> (literally, "Party of Liberation"). It is particularly strong in Central Asia and Europe and is growing in strength in the Arab world. It is based on the claim that Muslims can prove that God exists<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> and that the Qur'an is the word of God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Hizb ut-Tahrir's stated strategy is a non-violent political and intellectual struggle.</p>
<p>In Southeast Asia, groups such as <a href="/wiki/Jemaah_Islamiyah" title="Jemaah Islamiyah">Jemaah Islamiyah</a> aimed to establish a Caliphate across <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brunei" title="Brunei">Brunei</a> and parts of <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="al-Qaeda.27s_Caliphate_goals">al-Qaeda's Caliphate goals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: al-Qaeda's Caliphate goals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda" title="Al-Qaeda">al-Qaeda</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda" title="Al-Qaeda">Al-Qaeda</a> has as one of its clearly stated goals the re-establishment of a caliphate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Its former leader, Osama bin Laden, called for Muslims to "establish the righteous caliphate of our umma".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda" title="Al-Qaeda">Al-Qaeda</a> chiefs released a statement in 2005, under which, in what they call "phase five" there will be "an <a href="/wiki/Islamic_state" title="Islamic state">Islamic state</a>, or caliphate".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> Al-Qaeda has named its Internet newscast from <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> "The Voice of the Caliphate".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> According to author and Egyptian native <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Wright" title="Lawrence Wright">Lawrence Wright</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ayman_al-Zawahiri" title="Ayman al-Zawahiri">Ayman al-Zawahiri</a>, bin Laden's mentor and al-Qaeda's second-in-command until 2011, once "sought to restore the caliphate... which had formally ended in 1924 following the dissolution of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> but which had not exercised real power since the thirteenth century." Zawahiri believes that once the caliphate is re-established, Egypt would become a rallying point for the rest of the Islamic world, leading the <i>jihad</i> against the West. "Then history would make a new turn, God willing", Zawahiri later wrote, "in the opposite direction against the empire of the United States and the world's Jewish government".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Opposition">Opposition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Opposition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Scholar <a href="/wiki/Olivier_Roy_(professor)" title="Olivier Roy (professor)">Olivier Roy</a> writes that "early on, Islamists replace the concept of the caliphate ... with that of the <a href="/wiki/Emir" title="Emir">emir</a>." There were a number of reasons including "that according to the classical authors, a caliph must be a member of the tribe of the Prophet (the <a href="/wiki/Quraysh" title="Quraysh">Quraysh</a>) ... moreover, caliphs ruled societies that the Islamists do not consider to have been Islamic (the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>)."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> This is not the view of the majority of Islamist groups, as both the Muslim Brotherhood and Hizb ut-Tahrir view the Ottoman state as a caliphate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government">Government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electing_or_appointing_a_Caliph">Electing or appointing a Caliph</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Electing or appointing a Caliph">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In his book <i>The Early Islamic Conquests</i> (1981), <a href="/wiki/Fred_Donner" title="Fred Donner">Fred Donner</a> argues that the standard Arabian practice during the early Caliphates was for the prominent men of a kinship group, or tribe, to gather after a leader's death and elect a leader from amongst themselves, although there was no specified procedure for this <a href="/wiki/Shura" title="Shura">shura</a>, or consultative assembly. Candidates were usually from the same lineage as the deceased leader, but they were not necessarily his sons. Capable men who would lead well were preferred over an ineffectual direct heir, as there was no basis in the majority Sunni view that the head of state or governor should be chosen based on lineage alone.</p>
<p>This argument is advanced by Sunni Muslims, who believe that Muhammad's companion Abu Bakr was elected by the community and that this was the proper procedure.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> They further argue that a caliph is ideally chosen by election or community consensus.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Traditionally, Sunni Muslim <a href="/wiki/Madhhab" title="Madhhab">madhhabs</a> all agreed that a Caliph must be a descendant of the Quraysh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Al-Baqillani" title="Al-Baqillani">Al-Baqillani</a> has said that the leader of the Muslims simply should be from the majority. The founder of the biggest Sunni legal school, <a href="/wiki/Abu_Hanifa" title="Abu Hanifa">Abu Hanifa</a>, also wrote that the Caliph must be chosen by the majority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2muslims_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-2muslims-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (December 2016)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sunni_belief">Sunni belief</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Sunni belief">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Following the death of Muhammad, a meeting took place at <a href="/wiki/Saqifah" title="Saqifah">Saqifah</a>. At that meeting, Abu Bakr was elected caliph by the Muslim community. Sunni Muslims developed the belief that the caliph is a temporal political ruler, appointed to rule within the bounds of Islamic law (Sharia). The job of adjudicating orthodoxy and Islamic law was left to <a href="/wiki/Ijtihad#Qualifications_of_a_mujtahid" title="Ijtihad">mujtahids</a>, legal specialists collectively called the <a href="/wiki/Ulama" title="Ulama">Ulama</a>. Many Muslims call the first four caliphs the Rashidun, meaning the Rightly-Guided, because they are believed to have followed the Qur'an and the <a href="/wiki/Sunnah" title="Sunnah">sunnah</a> (example) of Muhammad.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shi.27a_belief">Shi'a belief</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Shi'a belief">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Succession_to_Muhammad" title="Succession to Muhammad">Succession to Muhammad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam" title="Shia Islam">Shia Islam</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Imamah_(Shia_doctrine)" title="Imamah (Shia doctrine)">Imamah (Shia doctrine)</a></div>
<p>The Shia believe in the <a href="/wiki/Imamah_(Shia_doctrine)" title="Imamah (Shia doctrine)">Imamate</a>, a principle by which rulers are Imams who are divinely chosen, infallible, and sinless and must come from the <a href="/wiki/Ahl_al-Bayt" title="Ahl al-Bayt">Ahl al-Bayt</a> regardless of majority opinion, <a href="/wiki/Shura" title="Shura">shura</a> or election. They claim that before his death, Muhammad had given many indications, in the <a href="/wiki/Hadith_of_the_pond_of_Khumm" title="Hadith of the pond of Khumm">hadith of the pond of Khumm</a> in particular, that he considered <a href="/wiki/Ali" title="Ali">Ali</a>, his cousin and son-in-law, as his successor. For the <a href="/wiki/Twelver" title="Twelver">Twelvers</a>, Ali and his eleven descendants, the twelve Imams, are believed to have been considered, even before their birth, as the only valid Islamic rulers appointed and decreed by God.</p>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Government:_Governance_of_the_Jurist" title="Islamic Government: Governance of the Jurist">Islamic Government: Governance of the Jurist</a></div>
<p>After these Twelve Imams, the potential Caliphs, had passed, and in the absence of the possibility of a government headed by their Imams, some Twelvers believe it was necessary that a system of Shi'i Islamic government based on the <a href="/wiki/Guardianship_of_the_Islamic_Jurist" title="Guardianship of the Islamic Jurist">Guardianship of the Islamic Jurist</a> be developed, due to the need for some form of government, where an Islamic jurist or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Faqih" title="Faqih">faqih</a> rules Muslims, suffices. However this idea, developed by the <a href="/wiki/Marja%27" title="Marja'">marja'</a> <a href="/wiki/Ayatollah" title="Ayatollah">Ayatollah</a> <a href="/wiki/Ruhollah_Khomeini" title="Ruhollah Khomeini">Ruhollah Khomeini</a> and established in Iran, is not universally accepted among the Shia.</p>
<p>Ismailis believe in the Imamate principle mentioned above, but they need not be secular rulers as well.</p>
<ul>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Nizari" title="Nizari">Nizari</a> continue to have a living Imam; the current Imam is the <a href="/wiki/Aga_Khan" title="Aga Khan">Aga Khan</a>.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Taiyabi_Ismaili" title="Taiyabi Ismaili">Taiyabi Ismaili</a> have, since the year 1130, followed the Imam's chief officer, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dai_al-Mutlaq" title="Dai al-Mutlaq">Dai al-Mutlaq</a>, as they believe the Imams are in a state of hiding.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Majlis_al-Shura_.28parliament.29"><i>Majlis al-Shura</i> (parliament)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Majlis al-Shura (parliament)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Majlis-ash-Shura" title="Majlis-ash-Shura">Majlis-ash-Shura</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shura" title="Shura">Shura</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Majlis" title="Majlis">Majlis</a></div>
<p>Traditional Sunni Islamic lawyers agree that <i>shura</i>, loosely translated as "consultation of the people", is a function of the caliphate. The <a href="/wiki/Majlis-ash-Shura" title="Majlis-ash-Shura">Majlis al-Shura</a> (literally "consultative assembly") or parliament was a representation of this idea of consultative governance. The importance of this is premised by the following verses of the Qur'an:</p>
<ul>
<li>"...those who answer the call of their Lord and establish the prayer; and who conduct their affairs by Shura [are loved by God]."<sup>[<a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/42:38" rel="nofollow">42:38</a>]</sup></li>
<li>"...consult them (the people) in their affairs. Then when you have taken a decision (from them), put your trust in Allah"<sup>[<a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/3:159" rel="nofollow">3:159</a>]</sup></li>
</ul>
<p>The majlis is also the means to elect a new caliph.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2muslims_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-2muslims-3">[3]</a></sup> Al-Mawardi has written that members of the majlis should satisfy three conditions: they must be just, have enough knowledge to distinguish a good caliph from a bad one, and have sufficient wisdom and judgment to select the best caliph. Al-Mawardi also said that in emergencies when there is no caliphate and no majlis, the people themselves should create a majlis and select a list of candidates for caliph; then the majlis should select a caliph from the list of candidates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2muslims_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-2muslims-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some Islamist interpretations of the role of the Majlis al-Shura are the following: In an analysis of the shura chapter of the Qur'an, Islamist author <a href="/wiki/Sayyid_Qutb" title="Sayyid Qutb">Sayyid Qutb</a> argues that Islam only requires the ruler to consult with some of the representatives of the ruled and govern within the context of the Sharia. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taqiuddin_al-Nabhani" title="Taqiuddin al-Nabhani">Taqiuddin al-Nabhani</a>, the founder of a transnational political movement devoted to the revival of the Caliphate, writes that although the Shura is an important part of "the ruling structure" of the Islamic caliphate, "(it is) not one of its pillars", meaning that its neglect would not make a Caliph's rule un-Islamic such as to justify a rebellion. However, the Muslim Brotherhood, the largest Islamic movement in Egypt, has toned down these Islamist views by accepting in principle that in the modern age the Majlis al-Shura is <a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">democracy</a> but during its governance of Egypt in 2013, the Muslim Brotherhood did not put that principle into practice.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Accountability_of_rulers">Accountability of rulers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Accountability of rulers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sunni Islamic lawyers have commented on when it is permissible to disobey, <a href="/wiki/Impeachment" title="Impeachment">impeach</a> or remove rulers in the Caliphate. This is usually when the rulers are not meeting their obligations to the public under Islam.</p>
<p>Al-Mawardi said that if the rulers meet their Islamic responsibilities to the public the people must obey their laws, but a Caliph or ruler who becomes either unjust or severely ineffective must be impeached via the Majlis al-Shura. Similarly, Al-Baghdadi<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (September 2008)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> believed that if the rulers do not uphold <a href="/wiki/Justice" title="Justice">justice</a>, the ummah via the majlis should warn them, and a Caliph who does not heed the warning can be impeached. <a href="/wiki/Al-Juwayni" title="Al-Juwayni">Al-Juwayni</a> argued that Islam is the goal of the ummah, so any ruler who deviates from this goal must be impeached. Al-Ghazali believed that <a href="/wiki/Oppression" title="Oppression">oppression</a> by a caliph is sufficient grounds for impeachment. Rather than just relying on impeachment, <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Hajar_al-Asqalani" title="Ibn Hajar al-Asqalani">Ibn Hajar al-Asqalani</a> stated that the people have an obligation to rebel if the caliph begins to act with no regard for Islamic law. Ibn Hajar al-Asqalani said that to ignore such a situation is <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haraam" title="Haraam">haraam</a></i> and those who cannot revolt from inside the caliphate should launch a struggle from outside. Al-Asqalani used two <a href="/wiki/Ayah" title="Ayah">ayahs</a> from the Qur'an to justify this:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>"...And they (the sinners on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_view_of_the_Last_Judgment" title="Islamic view of the Last Judgment">qiyama</a>) will say, 'Our Lord! We obeyed our leaders and our chiefs, and they misled us from the right path. Our Lord! Give them (the leaders) double the punishment you give us and curse them with a very great curse'..."<sup>[<a class="external text" href="https://quran.com/33:67" rel="nofollow">33:67–68</a>]</sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Islamic lawyers commented that when the rulers refuse to step down after being impeached through the Majlis, becoming dictators through the support of a corrupt army, if the majority is in agreement they have the option to launch a <a href="/wiki/Revolution" title="Revolution">revolution</a>. Many noted that this option is to be exercised only after factoring in the potential cost of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2muslims_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-2muslims-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rule_of_law">Rule of law</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Rule of law">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Sharia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islamic_ethics" title="Islamic ethics">Islamic ethics</a></div>
<p>The following <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">hadith</a> establishes the principle of <a href="/wiki/Rule_of_law" title="Rule of law">rule of law</a> in relation to <a href="/wiki/Nepotism" title="Nepotism">nepotism</a> and accountability<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Narrated ‘<a href="/wiki/Aisha" title="Aisha">Aisha</a>: The people of Quraish worried about the lady from <a href="/wiki/Banu_Makhzum" title="Banu Makhzum">Bani Makhzum</a> who had committed theft. They asked, "Who will intercede for her with Allah's Apostle?" Some said, "No one dare to do so except Usama bin Zaid the beloved one to Allah's Apostle." When Usama spoke about that to Allah's Apostle; Allah's Apostle said: "Do you try to intercede for somebody in a case connected with Allah’s Prescribed Punishments?" Then he got up and delivered a sermon saying, "What destroyed the nations preceding you, was that if a noble amongst them stole, they would forgive him, and if a poor person amongst them stole, they would inflict Allah's Legal punishment on him. By Allah, if <a href="/wiki/Fatimah" title="Fatimah">Fatima</a>, the daughter of Muhammad (my daughter) stole, I would cut off her hand."</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Various Islamic lawyers, however, place multiple conditions and stipulations on the execution of such a law, making it difficult to implement. For example, the poor cannot be penalized for stealing out of poverty, and during a time of drought in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rashidun_Empire" title="Rashidun Empire">Rashidun caliphate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Capital_punishment" title="Capital punishment">capital punishment</a> was suspended until the effects of the drought passed.</p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ulema" title="Ulema">Islamic jurists</a> later formulated the concept that all classes were subject to the law of the land, and no person is above the law; <a href="/wiki/Official" title="Official">officials</a> and private <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Citizen" title="Citizen">citizens</a> alike have a <a href="/wiki/Duty" title="Duty">duty</a> to obey the same law. Furthermore, a <a href="/wiki/Qadi" title="Qadi">Qadi</a> (Islamic judge) was not allowed to discriminate on the grounds of <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religion</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Race_(classification_of_human_beings)" title="Race (classification of human beings)">race</a>, <a href="/wiki/Human_skin_color" title="Human skin color">colour</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kinship" title="Kinship">kinship</a> or <a href="/wiki/Prejudice" title="Prejudice">prejudice</a>. In a number of cases, Caliphs had to appear before judges as they prepared to render their verdict.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to Noah Feldman, a law professor at <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a>, the system of legal scholars and jurists responsible for the <a href="/wiki/Rule_of_law" title="Rule of law">rule of law</a> was replaced by the <a href="/wiki/Codification_(law)" title="Codification (law)">codification</a> of Sharia by the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> in the early 19th century:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feldman_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Feldman-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_economics_in_the_world" title="Islamic economics in the world">Islamic economics in the world</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_capitalism" title="Islamic capitalism">Islamic capitalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bayt_al-mal" title="Bayt al-mal">Bayt al-mal</a></div>
<p>During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_Agricultural_Revolution" title="Muslim Agricultural Revolution">Muslim Agricultural Revolution</a>, the Caliphate understood that real <a href="/wiki/Incentive" title="Incentive">incentives</a> were needed to increase <a href="/wiki/Productivity" title="Productivity">productivity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wealth" title="Wealth">wealth</a> and thus enhance <a href="/wiki/Tax_revenue" title="Tax revenue">tax revenues</a>. A social transformation took place as a result of changing land <a href="/wiki/Ownership" title="Ownership">ownership</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Idrisi_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Idrisi-97">[97]</a></sup> giving individuals of any <a href="/wiki/Gender" title="Gender">gender</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethnic" title="Ethnic">ethnic</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religious" title="Religious">religious</a> background the right to buy, sell, <a href="/wiki/Mortgage_law" title="Mortgage law">mortgage</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Inheritance" title="Inheritance">inherit</a> land for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Farming" title="Farming">farming</a> or any other purpose. Based on the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs additional references to reliable sources. (December 2016)">additional citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> signatures were required on <a href="/wiki/Contract" title="Contract">contracts</a> for every major <a href="/wiki/Financial_transaction" title="Financial transaction">financial transaction</a> concerning <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Industry" title="Industry">industry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Commerce" title="Commerce">commerce</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Employment" title="Employment">employment</a>. Copies of the contract were usually kept by both parties involved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Idrisi_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-Idrisi-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are similarities between Islamic economics and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leftist" title="Leftist">leftist</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Socialist" title="Socialist">socialist</a> economic policies. Islamic jurists have argued that privatization of the origin of oil, gas, and other fire-producing fuels, agricultural land, and water is forbidden. The principle of public or joint ownership has been drawn by Muslim jurists from the following <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">hadith</a> of Muhammad:</p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ibn_Abbas" title="Ibn Abbas">Ibn Abbas</a> reported that the Messenger of Allah said: <i>"All Muslims are partners in three things- in water, herbage and fire."</i> (Narrated in Abu Daud, &amp; Ibn Majah)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> Anas added to the above hadith, <i>"Its price is Haram (forbidden)."</i><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Jurists have argued by <a href="/wiki/Qiyas" title="Qiyas">qiyas</a> that the above restriction on privatization can be extended to all essential resources that benefit the community as a whole.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Aside from similarities to socialism, early forms of proto-<a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">capitalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Free_market" title="Free market">free markets</a> were present in the Caliphate,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> since an early <a href="/wiki/Market_economy" title="Market economy">market economy</a> and early form of <a href="/wiki/Merchant_capitalism" title="Merchant capitalism">merchant capitalism</a> developed between the 8th and 12th centuries, which some refer to as "Islamic capitalism".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> A vigorous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monetary_economy" title="Monetary economy">monetary economy</a> developed based on the <a href="/wiki/List_of_circulating_currencies" title="List of circulating currencies">circulation</a> of a stable high-value <a href="/wiki/Currency" title="Currency">currency</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Dinar" title="Dinar">dinar</a>) and the integration of previously independent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monetary" title="Monetary">monetary</a> areas. Business techniques and forms of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Business_organization" title="Business organization">business organization</a> employed during this time included early <a href="/wiki/Contract" title="Contract">contracts</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bills_of_exchange" title="Bills of exchange">bills of exchange</a>, long-distance <a href="/wiki/International_trade" title="International trade">international trade</a>, early forms of <a href="/wiki/Partnership" title="Partnership">partnership</a> (<i>mufawada</i>) such as <a href="/wiki/Limited_partnership" title="Limited partnership">limited partnerships</a> (<i>mudaraba</i>), and early forms of <a href="/wiki/Credit_(finance)" title="Credit (finance)">credit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Debt" title="Debt">debt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Profit_(accounting)" title="Profit (accounting)">profit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Income_statement" title="Income statement">loss</a>, <a href="/wiki/Capital_(economics)" title="Capital (economics)">capital</a> (<i>al-mal</i>), <a href="/wiki/Capital_accumulation" title="Capital accumulation">capital accumulation</a> (<i>nama al-mal</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Banaji_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Banaji-102">[102]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Circulating_capital" title="Circulating capital">circulating capital</a>, <a href="/wiki/Capital_expenditure" title="Capital expenditure">capital expenditure</a>, <a href="/wiki/Revenue" title="Revenue">revenue</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cheque" title="Cheque">cheques</a>, <a href="/wiki/Promissory_note" title="Promissory note">promissory notes</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trusts" title="Trusts">trusts</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Waqf" title="Waqf">waqf</a></i>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Startup_companies" title="Startup companies">startup companies</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Savings_account" title="Savings account">savings accounts</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transactional_account" title="Transactional account">transactional accounts</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pawnshop" title="Pawnshop">pawning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Loan" title="Loan">loaning</a>, <a href="/wiki/Exchange_rate" title="Exchange rate">exchange rates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bank" title="Bank">bankers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Money_changer" title="Money changer">money changers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ledger" title="Ledger">ledgers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deposit_account" title="Deposit account">deposits</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assignment_(law)" title="Assignment (law)">assignments</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Double-entry_bookkeeping_system" title="Double-entry bookkeeping system">double-entry bookkeeping system</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Lawsuit" title="Lawsuit">lawsuits</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Organization" title="Organization">Organizational</a> <a href="/wiki/Business" title="Business">enterprises</a> similar to <a href="/wiki/Corporation" title="Corporation">corporations</a> independent from the <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">state</a> also existed in the medieval Islamic world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> Many of these concepts were adopted and further advanced in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval_Europe" title="Medieval Europe">medieval Europe</a> from the 13th century onwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Banaji_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-Banaji-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>The concepts of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Welfare_(financial_aid)" title="Welfare (financial aid)">welfare</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pension" title="Pension">pension</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> were introduced in early <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Islamic law</a> as forms of <i><a href="/wiki/Zakat" title="Zakat">Zakat</a></i> (charity), one of the <a href="/wiki/Five_Pillars_of_Islam" title="Five Pillars of Islam">Five Pillars of Islam</a>, since the time of the <a href="/wiki/Rashidun_Caliphate" title="Rashidun Caliphate">Rashidun caliph</a> <a href="/wiki/Umar" title="Umar">Umar</a> in the 7th century. The <a href="/wiki/Tax" title="Tax">taxes</a> (including <i>Zakat</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Jizya" title="Jizya">Jizya</a></i>) collected in the <a href="/wiki/Treasury" title="Treasury">treasury</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Bayt_al-mal" title="Bayt al-mal">Bayt al-mal</a></i>) of an Islamic <a href="/wiki/Government" title="Government">government</a> were used to provide <a href="/wiki/Income" title="Income">income</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Poverty" title="Poverty">needy</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Poverty" title="Poverty">poor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Old_age" title="Old age">elderly</a>, <a href="/wiki/Orphan" title="Orphan">orphans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Widow" title="Widow">widows</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Disability" title="Disability">disabled</a>.</p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Demographics" title="Demographics">demographics</a> of medieval Islamic society varied in some significant aspects from other agricultural societies, including a decline in <a href="/wiki/Birth_rate" title="Birth rate">birth rates</a> as well as a change in <a href="/wiki/Life_expectancy" title="Life expectancy">life expectancy</a>. Other traditional agrarian societies are estimated to have had an average life expectancy of 20 to 25 years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">ancient Rome</a> and <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">medieval Europe</a> are estimated at 20 to 30 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> The life expectancy of Islamic society diverged from that of other traditional agrarian societies, with several studies on the lifespans of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ulema" title="Ulema">Islamic scholars</a> concluding that members of this occupational group enjoyed a life expectancy between 69 and 75 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> Such studies have given the following estimates for the average lifespans of religious scholars at various times and places: 72.8 years in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>, 69–75 years in 11th century <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Islamic Spain</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> 75 years in 12th century Persia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> and 59–72 years in 13th century Persia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ahmad_2007_246.E2.80.93248_246_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ahmad_2007_246.E2.80.93248_246-114">[114]</a></sup> However, Maya Shatzmiller considers these religious scholars to be a misleading sample who are not representative of the general population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> Conrad I. Lawrence estimates the average lifespan in the early Islamic Caliphate to be above 35 years for the general population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>The early Islamic Empire also had the highest literacy rates among pre-modern societies, alongside the city of <a href="/wiki/Classical_Athens" title="Classical Athens">classical Athens</a> in the 4th century BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> and later, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> after the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Printing" title="Printing">printing</a> from the 10th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> One factor for the relatively high literacy rates in the early Islamic Empire was its parent-driven educational marketplace, as the state did not systematically subsidize educational services until the introduction of state funding under <a href="/wiki/Nizam_al-Mulk" title="Nizam al-Mulk">Nizam al-Mulk</a> in the 11th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> Another factor was the diffusion of <a href="/wiki/Paper" title="Paper">paper</a> from China,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> which led to an efflorescence of books and written culture in Islamic society; thus <a href="/wiki/Papermaking" title="Papermaking">papermaking</a> technology transformed Islamic society (and later, the rest of <a href="/wiki/Afro-Eurasia" title="Afro-Eurasia">Afro-Eurasia</a>) from an <a href="/wiki/Speech" title="Speech">oral</a> to <a href="/wiki/Scribe" title="Scribe">scribal</a> culture, comparable to the later shifts from scribal to <a href="/wiki/Typography" title="Typography">typographic</a> culture, and from typographic culture to the <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">Internet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> Other factors include the widespread use of paper <a href="/wiki/Book" title="Book">books</a> in Islamic society (more so than any other previously existing society), the study <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hafiz_(Qur%27an)" title="Hafiz (Qur'an)">and memorization</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qur%27an" title="Qur'an">Qur'an</a>, flourishing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_economics_in_the_world" title="Islamic economics in the world">commercial activity</a>, and the emergence of the <a href="/wiki/Maktab" title="Maktab">Maktab</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Madrasah" title="Madrasah">Madrasah</a> educational institutions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_caliphs">Notable caliphs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Caliphate&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Notable caliphs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">For a more comprehensive list, see <a href="/wiki/List_of_Caliphs" title="List of Caliphs">List of Caliphs</a>.</div>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Rashidun" title="Rashidun">Rashidun</a></i> ("Righteously Guided")
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr" title="Abu Bakr">Abu Bakr</a>, first Rashidun Caliph. Subdued rebel tribes in the <a href="/wiki/Ridda_wars" title="Ridda wars">Ridda wars</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Umar" title="Umar">Umar</a> (Umar ibn al-Khattab), second Rashidun Caliph. During his reign, the Islamic empire expanded to include Egypt, Jerusalem and Persia.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uthman_Ibn_Affan" title="Uthman Ibn Affan">Uthman Ibn Affan</a>, third Rashidun Caliph. The various written copies of the Qur'an were standardized under his direction. Killed by rebels.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ali" title="Ali">Ali</a> (Ali ibn Abu Talib), fourth Rashidun Caliph. Considered by Shi'a Muslims however to be the first <a href="/wiki/Imam" title="Imam">Imam</a>. His reign was fraught with internal conflict, with Muawiyah ibn Abi Sufyan (Muawiyah I) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amr_ibn_al-As" title="Amr ibn al-As">Amr ibn al-As</a> controlling the Levant and Egypt regions independently of Ali.</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hasan_ibn_Ali" title="Hasan ibn Ali">Hasan ibn Ali</a>, fifth Caliph. Considered as "rightly guided" by several historians. He abdicated his right to the caliphate in favour of Muawiyah I in order to end the potential for ruinous civil war.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Muawiyah_I" title="Muawiyah I">Muawiyah I</a>, first caliph of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Umayyad_dynasty" title="Umayyad dynasty">Umayyad dynasty</a>. Muawiyah instituted dynastic rule by appointing his son <a href="/wiki/Yazid_I" title="Yazid I">Yazid I</a> as his successor, a trend that would continue through subsequent caliphates.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Umar_ibn_Abd_al-Aziz" title="Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz">Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz</a> (Umar II), Umayyad caliph who is considered one of the finest rulers in Muslim history. He is also considered by some (mainly Sunnis) to be among the "rightly guided" caliphs.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Harun_al-Rashid" title="Harun al-Rashid">Harun al-Rashid</a>, Abbasid caliph during whose reign Baghdad became the world's prominent centre of trade, learning, and culture. Harun is the subject of many stories in the famous <i><a href="/wiki/One_Thousand_and_One_Nights" title="One Thousand and One Nights">One Thousand and One Nights</a></i>. He established the legendary library <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bayt_al-Hikma" title="Bayt al-Hikma">Bayt al-Hikma</a> ("House of Wisdom").</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Al-Ma%27mun" title="Al-Ma'mun">Al-Ma'mun</a>, a great Abbasid patron of Islamic philosophy and science.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mehmed_II" title="Mehmed II">Mehmed II</a>, an Ottoman caliph who brought an end to the Byzantine Empire.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Suleiman_the_Magnificent" title="Suleiman the Magnificent">Suleiman the Magnificent</a>, an Ottoman caliph during whose reign the Ottoman Empire reached its zenith.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Abdul_Hamid_II" title="Abdul Hamid II">Abdul Hamid II</a>, last Ottoman caliph to rule with independent, absolute power.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Abd%C3%BClmecid_II" title="Abdülmecid II">Abdülmecid II</a>, last caliph of the Ottoman dynasty. Nominally the 37th Head of the Ottoman dynasty.</li>
</ul>

<li id="cite_note-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-10">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Uthman was the son of <a href="/wiki/Affan_ibn_Abi_al-%27As" title="Affan ibn Abi al-'As">`Affan</a>, the son of <a href="/wiki/Abu_al-%27As_ibn_Umayyah" title="Abu al-'As ibn Umayyah">Abu-l`As</a>, the son of <a href="/wiki/Umayya_ibn_Abd_Shams" title="Umayya ibn Abd Shams">Umayya ibn Abd Shams</a>. Mu'awiyah was the son of <a href="/wiki/Abu_Sufyan_ibn_Harb" title="Abu Sufyan ibn Harb">Abu Sufyan</a>, the son of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Harb_ibn_Umayya&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Harb ibn Umayya (page does not exist)">Harb</a>, the son of Umayya ibn Abd Shams.</span></li>
