"The Arab Nation: Some Conclusions and Problems". <i>MERIP Reports</i>. <b>68</b>: 3–14. <a href="/wiki/JSTOR" title="JSTOR">JSTOR</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.jstor.org/stable/3011226" rel="nofollow">3011226</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACaliphate&amp;rft.atitle=The+Arab+Nation%3A+Some+Conclusions+and+Problems&amp;rft.aufirst=Samir&amp;rft.aulast=Amin&amp;rft.date=1978-06&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.jstor.org%2Fstable%2F3011226&amp;rft.jtitle=MERIP+Reports&amp;rft.pages=3-14&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=68"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-109"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-109">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation" id="CITEREFShatzmiller1994">Shatzmiller, Maya (1994), <i>Labour in the Medieval Islamic World</i>, Brill Publishers, pp. 63–4 &amp; 66, <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/90-04-09896-8" title="Special:BookSources/90-04-09896-8">90-04-09896-8</a>, <q>At the same time, the “demographic behaviour” of the Islamic society as an agricultural society varied in some significant aspects from other agricultural societies, particularly in ways which could explain a decline in birth rate. It is agreed that all agricultural societies conform to a given demographic pattern of behaviour, which includes a high birth-rate and a slightly lower death-rate, significant enough to allow a slow population increase of 0.5 to 1.0 per cent per year. Other demographic characteristics of this society are high infant mortality, with 200–500 deaths per 1000 within the first year of birth, a lower average life expectancy, of twenty to twenty-five years, and a broadly based population pyramid, where the number of young people at the bottom of the pyramid is very high in relationship to the rest of the population, and that children are set to work at an early stage. Islamic society diverged from this demographic profile in some significant points, although not always consistently. Studies have shown that during certain periods, such factors as attitudes to marriage and sex, birth control, birth and death rates, age of marriage and patterns of marriage, family size and migration pattems, varied from the traditional agricultural model. [...] Life expectancy was another area where Islamic society diverged from the suggested model for agricultural society.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACaliphate&amp;rft.aufirst=Maya&amp;rft.aulast=Shatzmiller&amp;rft.btitle=Labour+in+the+Medieval+Islamic+World&amp;rft.date=1994&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=90-04-09896-8&amp;rft.pages=63-4+%26+66&amp;rft.pub=Brill+Publishers&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-110"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-110">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation"><a class="external text" href="http://www.britannica.com/eb/topic-340119/life-expectancy" rel="nofollow">"Life expectancy (sociology)"</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica" title="Encyclopædia Britannica">Encyclopædia Britannica</a></i><span class="reference-accessdate">, retrieved <span class="nowrap">2010-04-17</span></span>, <q>In ancient Rome and medieval Europe the average life span is estimated to have been between 20 and 30 years.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACaliphate&amp;rft.atitle=Life+expectancy+%28sociology%29&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.britannica.com%2Feb%2Ftopic-340119%2Flife-expectancy&amp;rft.jtitle=Encyclop%C3%A6dia+Britannica&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-111"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-111">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation" id="CITEREFShatzmiller1994">Shatzmiller, Maya (1994), <i>Labour in the Medieval Islamic World</i>, Brill Publishers, p. 66, <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/90-04-09896-8" title="Special:BookSources/90-04-09896-8">90-04-09896-8</a>, <q>Life expectancy was another area where Islamic society diverged from the suggested model for agricultural society. No less than three separate studies about the life expectancy of religious scholars, two from 11th century Muslim Spain, and one from the Middle East, concluded that members of this occupational group enjoyed a life expectancy of 69, 75, and 72.8 years respectively!</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACaliphate&amp;rft.aufirst=Maya&amp;rft.aulast=Shatzmiller&amp;rft.btitle=Labour+in+the+Medieval+Islamic+World&amp;rft.date=1994&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=90-04-09896-8&amp;rft.pages=66&amp;rft.pub=Brill+Publishers&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-115"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-115">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation" id="CITEREFShatzmiller1994">Shatzmiller, Maya (1994), <i>Labour in the Medieval Islamic World</i>, Brill Publishers, p. 66, <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/90-04-09896-8" title="Special:BookSources/90-04-09896-8">90-04-09896-8</a>, <q>This rate is uncommonly high, not only under the conditions in medieval cities, where these ‘ulama’ lived, but also in terms of the average life expectancy for contemporary males. <li id="cite_note-117"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-117">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation" id="CITEREFAndrew_J._Coulson">Andrew J. Coulson, <a class="external text" href="http://media.hoover.org/documents/0817928928_105.pdf" rel="nofollow"><i>Delivering Education</i></a> <span style="font-size:85%;">(PDF)</span>, <a href="/wiki/Hoover_Institution" title="Hoover Institution">Hoover Institution</a>, p. 117<span class="reference-accessdate">, retrieved <span class="nowrap">2008-11-22</span></span>, <q>Reaching further back through the centuries, the civilizations regarded as having the highest literacy rates of their ages were parent-driven educational marketplaces. The ability to read and write was far more widely enjoyed in the early medieval Islamic empire and in fourth-century-B.C.E. Athens than in any other cultures of their times.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACaliphate&amp;rft.au=Andrew+J.+Coulson&amp;rft.btitle=Delivering+Education&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fmedia.hoover.org%2Fdocuments%2F0817928928_105.pdf&amp;rft.pages=117&amp;rft.pub=Hoover+Institution&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-118"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-118">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation" id="CITEREFEdmund_Burke2009">Edmund Burke (June 2009), "Islam at the Center: Technological Complexes and the Roots of Modernity", <i><a href="/wiki/Journal_of_World_History" title="Journal of World History">Journal of World History</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Hawaii_Press" title="University of Hawaii Press">University of Hawaii Press</a>, <b>20</b> (2): 165–186 [177–8], <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.1353%2Fjwh.0.0045" rel="nofollow">10.1353/jwh.0.0045</a>, <q>The spread of written knowledge was at least the equal of what it was in China after printing became common there in the tenth century. (We should note that Chinese books were printed in small editions of a hundred or so copies.)</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACaliphate&amp;rft.atitle=Islam+at+the+Center%3A+Technological+Complexes+and+the+Roots+of+Modernity&amp;rft.au=Edmund+Burke&amp;rft.date=2009-06&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1353%2Fjwh.0.0045&amp;rft.issue=2&amp;rft.jtitle=Journal+of+World+History&amp;rft.pages=165-186+177-8&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=20"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-119"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-119">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation" id="CITEREFAndrew_J._Coulson">Andrew J. Coulson, <a class="external text" href="http://media.hoover.org/documents/0817928928_105.pdf" rel="nofollow"><i>Delivering Education</i></a> <span style="font-size:85%;">(PDF)</span>, <a href="/wiki/Hoover_Institution" title="Hoover Institution">Hoover Institution</a>, p. 117<span class="reference-accessdate">, retrieved <span class="nowrap">2008-11-22</span></span>, <q>In neither case did the state supply or even systematically subsidize educational services. The Muslim world’s eventual introduction of state funding under Nizam al-Mulk in the eleventh century was quickly followed by partisan religious squabbling over education and the gradual fall of Islam from its place of cultural and scientific preeminence.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACaliphate&amp;rft.au=Andrew+J.+Coulson&amp;rft.btitle=Delivering+Education&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fmedia.hoover.org%2Fdocuments%2F0817928928_105.pdf&amp;rft.pages=117&amp;rft.pub=Hoover+Institution&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-120"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-120">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation" id="CITEREFEdmund_Burke2009">Edmund Burke (June 2009), "Islam at the Center: Technological Complexes and the Roots of Modernity", <i><a href="/wiki/Journal_of_World_History" title="Journal of World History">Journal of World History</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Hawaii_Press" title="University of Hawaii Press">University of Hawaii Press</a>, <b>20</b> (2): 165–186 [177], <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.1353%2Fjwh.0.0045" rel="nofollow">10.1353/jwh.0.0045</a>, <q>According to legend, paper came to the Islamic world as a result of the capture of Chinese paper makers at the 751 C.E. battle of Talas River.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACaliphate&amp;rft.atitle=Islam+at+the+Center%3A+Technological+Complexes+and+the+Roots+of+Modernity&amp;rft.au=Edmund+Burke&amp;rft.date=2009-06&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1353%2Fjwh.0.0045&amp;rft.issue=2&amp;rft.jtitle=Journal+of+World+History&amp;rft.pages=165-186+177&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=20"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-121"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-121">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation" id="CITEREFEdmund_Burke2009">Edmund Burke (June 2009), "Islam at the Center: Technological Complexes and the Roots of Modernity", <i><a href="/wiki/Journal_of_World_History" title="Journal of World History">Journal of World History</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Hawaii_Press" title="University of Hawaii Press">University of Hawaii Press</a>, <b>20</b> (2): 165–186 [177], <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.1353%2Fjwh.0.0045" rel="nofollow">10.1353/jwh.0.0045</a>, <q>Whatever the source, the diffusion of paper-making technology via the lands of Islam produced a shift from oral to scribal culture across the rest of Afroeurasia that was rivaled only by the move from scribal to typographic culture. (Perhaps it will prove to have been even more important than the recent move from typographic culture to the Internet.) The result was remarkable. As historian Jonathan Bloom informs us, paper encouraged "an efflorescence of books and written culture incomparably more brilliant than was known anywhere in Europe until the invention of printing with movable type in the fifteenth century.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACaliphate&amp;rft.atitle=Islam+at+the+Center%3A+Technological+Complexes+and+the+Roots+of+Modernity&amp;rft.au=Edmund+Burke&amp;rft.date=2009-06&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1353%2Fjwh.0.0045&amp;rft.issue=2&amp;rft.jtitle=Journal+of+World+History&amp;rft.pages=165-186+177&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=20"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-122"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-122">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation" id="CITEREFEdmund_Burke2009">Edmund Burke (June 2009), "Islam at the Center: Technological Complexes and the Roots of Modernity", <i><a href="/wiki/Journal_of_World_History" title="Journal of World History">Journal of World History</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Hawaii_Press" title="University of Hawaii Press">University of Hawaii Press</a>, <b>20</b> (2): 165–186 [178], <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.1353%2Fjwh.0.0045" rel="nofollow">10.1353/jwh.0.0045</a>, <q>More so than any previously existing society, Islamic society of the period 1000–1500 was profoundly a culture of books. [...] The emergence of a culture of books is closely tied to cultural dispositions toward literacy in Islamic societies. Muslim young men were encouraged to memorize the Qur'an as part of their transition to adulthood, and while most presumably did not (though little is known about literacy levels in pre-Mongol Muslim societies), others did. Types of literacy in any event varied, as Nelly Hanna has recently suggested, and are best studied as part of the complex social dynamics and contexts of individual Muslim societies. The need to conform commercial contracts and business arrangements to <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Islamic law</a> provided a further impetus for literacy, especially likely in commercial centers. Scholars often engaged in commercial activity and craftsmen or tradesmen often spent time studying in madrasas. The connection between what <a href="/wiki/Brian_Street" title="Brian Street">Brian Street</a> has called "maktab literacy" and commercial literacy was real and exerted a steady pressure on individuals to upgrade their reading skills.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACaliphate&amp;rft.atitle=Islam+at+the+Center%3A+Technological+Complexes+and+the+Roots+of+Modernity&amp;rft.au=Edmund+Burke&amp;rft.date=2009-06&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1353%2Fjwh.0.0045&amp;rft.issue=2&amp;rft.jtitle=Journal+of+World+History&amp;rft.pages=165-186+178&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=20"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></span></li>
</ol>
</div>
